    <title>Helena Carroll</title>
    <ns>0</ns>
    <id>2336433</id>

      <text xml:space="preserve">{{distinguish|Helen Carroll}}
{{Use British English|date=April 2014}}
{{More footnotes|date=April 2013}}
{{Use dmy dates|date=April 2014}}
{{Infobox person
| name                  = Helena Carroll
| image                 = 
| imagesize             =  
| caption               = 
| birthname             = Helena Winifred Carroll
| birth_date            = {{Birth_date|1928|11|13|df=yes}}
| birth_place           = [[Glasgow, Scotland]], UK 
| death_date            = {{death_date and age|2013|3|31|1928|11|13|df=yes}}
| death_place           = [[Los Angeles, California]], U.S.
| occupation            = Actress
| years_active          = 1950-2013}}
'''Helena Winifred Carroll''' (13 November 1928 – 31 March 2013) was a veteran film, television and stage actress. Born to clothing designer Helena Reilly and [[Abbey Theatre]] playwright [[Paul Vincent Carroll]], she was the youngest of three sisters. Her elder sisters were Theresa Elizabeth Perez (1924-2001), a classically trained musician and the producer/founder of the ''People's Pops Concerts'' in [[Phoenix, Arizona]], and journalist Kathleen Moira Carroll (1927-2007). 
Miss Carroll attended Clerkhill [[Notre Dame High School (Glasgow)|Notre Dame High School]], a Roman Catholic convent school in [[Dumbarton]]. She received her training at the Central School which later became the [[Webber Douglas Academy of Dramatic Art]] London, appearing in three plays in [[London's West End]] and a film, ''[[Midnight Episode]]'', by age 20. She made her Broadway debut in ''[[Separate Tables]]'' by [[Terence Rattigan]]. She moved to the U.S. during the 1950s, touring and performing on Broadway and co-founded, with Dermot McNamara, {{Who?|date=April 2013}} The Irish Players, a repertory theater company in Manhattan.&lt;ref&gt;[https://www.nytimes.com/2013/04/11/movies/helena-carroll-actress-and-irish-players-co-founder-dies-at-84.html?ref=obituaries Obituary for Helena Carroll], ''New York Times'', 11 April 2013.&lt;/ref&gt;
At the dawn of television, Miss Carroll played the leading role of Nora, in a television production of her father's play, ''[[The White Steed]]'' (1959 Play of the Week Series), directed by [[Joe Gisterak]]. Gisterak directed a 1980 commissioned opera of her father's play, ''Beauty is Fled'', as part of the &quot;Children's Opera Series&quot;, which her sister,  Theresa Perez founded. The opera was performed at the [[Phoenix Symphony Hall]]. 
Helena split her stage work between Dublin, London and New York, appearing on Broadway in, among other productions the original production of ''[[Oliver!]]'' as Mrs. Sowerberry, as well as ''[[Pickwick (musical)|Pickwick]]'', ''[[Design for Living]]'', ''[[Waiting in the Wings (play)|Waiting in the Wings]]'', and the [[Elizabeth Taylor]]-[[Richard Burton]] revivals of ''[[Private Lives]]'' (New York and Los Angeles).&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.ibdb.com/person.php?id=73575 Helena Carroll's IBDb profile]; accessed 13 April 2014.&lt;/ref&gt; 
Prompted by friends who had preceded her to Los Angeles, producer Al Simon and casting director Caro Jones (the latter of whom gave Les Moonves, now president of CBS, his first job in the biz), Carroll moved to Los Angeles in the late 1960s and appeared in numerous films and television programs, including [[John Huston]]'s [[Academy Award]] nominated film ''[[The Dead (short story)|The Dead]]'', ''[[The Friends of Eddie Coyle]]'' starring [[Robert Mitchum]], ''[[The Jerk (film)|The Jerk]]'', directed by [[Carl Reiner]] and starring [[Steve Martin]], ''[[The Mambo Kings]]'', the [[Warren Beatty]] remake of ''[[Love Affair (1994 film)|Love Affair]]'', and such television programs as ''[[General Hospital]]'', ''[[Edge of Night]]'', ''[[Loving Couples (1980 film)|Loving Couples]]'', ''[[Laverne and Shirley]]'', ''[[Murder She Wrote]]'', etc.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.imdb.com/name/nm0140833 Helena Carroll's IMDb profile]; accessed 13 April 2014.&lt;/ref&gt;
==Death==
Carroll resided in Los Angeles, and died in [[Marina del Rey, California]] from heart failure on 31 March 2013 at the age of 84. Her last stage performance was in 2007 at the age of 78. She is survived by a half brother, Brian Carroll; a niece, Helena Perez Reilly; and a great-nephew, Paul Vincent Reilly.&lt;ref&gt;[http://variety.com/2013/legit/people-news/obituaries-people-news/irish-thesp-helena-carroll-dies-at-84-1200332626 Obituary for Helena Carroll in ''Variety'']; accessed 13 April 2014.&lt;/ref&gt;

    <title>Mario Sammarco</title>
    <ns>0</ns>
    <id>2336437</id>
    <revision>

      <parentid>660399684</parentid>
      <timestamp>2016-02-17T15:00:39Z</timestamp>
      <contributor>
        <username>KasparBot</username>

      </contributor>
      <comment>migrating [[Wikipedia:Persondata|Persondata]] to Wikidata, [[toollabs:kasparbot/persondata/|please help]], see [[toollabs:kasparbot/persondata/challenge.php/article/Mario Sammarco|challenges for this article]]</comment>

      <format>text/x-wiki</format>
      <text xml:space="preserve">[[File:Mario Sammarco cph.3a40624.jpg|thumb|Mario Sammarco in costume]]
'''(Giuseppe) Mario Sammarco''' (December 13, 1868, although some sources say 1867 – January 24, 1930) was an Italian operatic [[baritone]] noted for his acting ability.
==Biography==
Sammarco was born in [[Palermo]], [[Sicily]], and studied locally with Antonio Cantelli. He made his operatic début in Palermo as Valentine in ''[[Faust (opera)|Faust]]'' in 1888. He subsequently sang to acclaim in [[Milan]] (at [[La Scala]], Italy's most celebrated theatre), [[Buenos Aires]] and London. Between 1904 and 1919 he appeared intermittently, in 26 different roles, at the [[Royal Opera House]], [[Covent Garden]]. 
In New York City, he was hired by [[Oscar Hammerstein I]] for his [[Manhattan Opera Company]] as a replacement for the great French singing-actor [[Maurice Renaud]]. He sang with the Manhattan company in 1908-1910, becoming its principal Italian baritone, but he never 'graduated' to the rival [[Metropolitan Opera]].   
Sammarco next joined the [[Chicago]]&amp;ndash;[[Philadelphia]] opera company. His career there continued smoothly enough until 1913 when he encountered a disapproving [[Mary Garden]] in a Chicago production of ''[[Tosca]]''. The soprano requested that he be replaced; but after he named some of his former distinguished (and uncomplaining) Tosca partners, notably [[Emmy Destinn]], the performances proceeded to be given to critical success.
His final operatic appearances were at the [[Teatro di San Carlo]] in Naples in 1919. 
Apart from Scarpia (his debut role at Covent Garden), he was a famous Rigoletto, Marcello, Germont, Renato, Enrico and Amleto, appearing opposite some of the finest singers of his day. No [[bel canto]] specialist, he was at his best in ardent [[verismo]] works, creating the roles of Gerard in [[Umberto Giordano|Giordano]]'s ''[[Andrea Chénier]]'' in 1896 and Cascart in [[Ruggiero Leoncavallo|Leoncavallo]]'s ''[[Zazà]]'' in 1900.
Sammarco was active during an era that was thronged with Italian baritones of exceptional ability. It was no small achievement for him to carve out a lucrative international career in the face of powerful competition from the likes of [[Mattia Battistini]], [[Antonio Magini-Coletti]], [[Giuseppe Campanari]], [[Mario Ancona]], Giuseppe Pacini, [[Antonio Scotti]], [[Eugenio Giraldoni]], [[Riccardo Stracciari]], [[Titta Ruffo]], [[Domenico Viglione Borghese]], [[Pasquale Amato]] and [[Carlo Galeffi]].       
He taught singing after retiring from the stage and died in Milan. 
==Recordings==
Sammarco possessed a strong voice with a powerful upper register; but of all the celebrated singers preserved on early recordings, Sammarco's are regarded as the most disappointing. The technical quality of his singing disappoints and the timbre of his voice can sound rough; at best he is merely dull. On record, according to Scott and Steane, he has a singular inability to sing less than mezzo forte and has no concept of legato. He impresses most when delivering declamatory passages in verismo operas (see Scott, cited below) including an early (celebrated or notorious) recording with the soprano Emma Carelli from Tosca, complete with peals of laughter; even in these &quot;specialist&quot; roles, his singing is unimaginative and crude. Many of the depressingly numerous 78-rpm [[gramophone record]]s that he made prior to [[World War I]] for the Fonotipia, Victor and Pathé companies—and for the forerunner of [[HMV]]—are now available on CD reissues from various labels.
==Sources==
*Celletti, Rodolfo  (1964). Le Grandi Voci. Istituto per la Collaborazione Culturale (Rome) 
*Kutsch, K. J. and Riemens, Leo (1969). A Biographical Dictionary of Singers. Chilton Book Company (New York)
*Warrack, John and West, Ewan (1992), ''The Oxford Dictionary of Opera'', 782 pages,  ISBN 0-19-869164-5
*Scott, Michael (1977), ''The Record of Singing'' (Volume One). Duckworth (London), 243 pages, ISBN 0-7156-1030-9

    <title>Colonial (PRR train)</title>
    <ns>0</ns>
    <id>2336439</id>

      <contributor>
        <username>Bender the Bot</username>

      <minor />
      <comment>/* History */http&amp;rarr;https for [[Google Books]] and [[Google News]] using [[Project:AWB|AWB]]</comment>
      <model>wikitext</model>
      <format>text/x-wiki</format>
      <text xml:space="preserve">{{italic title}}
The '''''Colonial''''', also known as the '''''Colonial Express''''', was a service of the [[Pennsylvania Railroad]] and [[New York, New Haven and Hartford Railroad]] between [[Union Station (DC)|Union Station]] in [[Washington, DC]] and [[South Station (Boston)|South Station]] in [[Boston, Massachusetts]]. It was operated until 1973 by [[Amtrak]].
==History==
The ''Colonial'' began on January 18, 1892, as a daytime express service between Boston and Washington. From Boston, the train traveled west over the New York, New Haven &amp; Hartford's [[Shore Line]] and then the New Rochelle branch to the [[Harlem River]]. There, passengers boarded the passenger steamer ''Maryland'', which carried them to [[Jersey City, New Jersey]]. At that time the Pennsylvania Railroad (PRR) had no station in Manhattan, so passengers heading for [[New York City|New York]] traveled to Jersey City, then transferred to Manhattan-bound ferries. Passengers continuing south on the ''Colonial'' boarded a new train in Jersey City, which used the Pennsylvania main line to reach [[Philadelphia, Pennsylvania]], [[Baltimore, Maryland]] and Washington.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news | title=The Colonial Express: Name and Route of the New Train Which Goes on Monday | date=January 14, 1892 | accessdate=2009-12-18 | publisher=''[[The Day (New London)|The Day]]'' | url=https://news.google.com/newspapers?id=n_AgAAAAIBAJ&amp;sjid=6XMFAAAAIBAJ&amp;pg=3183,906859&amp;dq=pennsylvania+railroad+colonial&amp;hl=en}}&lt;/ref&gt;
The sinking of the ocean liner [[RMS Titanic|RMS ''Titanic'']] caused a shift in the ''Colonial'''s operation. The waterborne segment was dropped; instead, the Boston train ran into [[Grand Central Terminal]], while the Washington train terminated at [[Pennsylvania Station (New York City)|Penn Station]], which had opened in 1910. Passengers used ground transport to move between the two stations, which were a little over a mile apart. In 1917 the opening of the [[Hell Gate Bridge]], which linked the PRR and the New Haven, allowed trains to run directly through Penn Station.&lt;ref name=&quot;lynch&quot;&gt;{{Lynch-New Haven passenger | page=29}}&lt;/ref&gt;
The PRR merged into [[Penn Central Transportation]] in 1968, which absorbed the NYNH&amp;H in 1969. When Amtrak began operations on May 1, 1971, it took over the ''Colonial'', which ran as trains 170 and 171 (changed to 173 and 174 in the November 14 timetable). Its last trip was made on April 28, 1973.
In 1976 Amtrak brought the name back for the New York-[[Newport News (VA)|Newport News]] ''[[Colonial (Amtrak 1976)|Colonial]]'' to replace the removed Newport News section of the ''[[James Whitcomb Riley (Amtrak)|James Whitcomb Riley]]''.

'''Mary Jo Catlett''' (born September 2, 1938) is an American film, stage and television actress. She is notable for her role as housekeeper Pearl Gallagher on the television sitcom ''[[Diff'rent Strokes]]'', and currently for her role as [[Mrs. Puff]] in ''[[SpongeBob SquarePants]]'', which she has held since the show's debut.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title= Mary Jo Catlett|url=http://www.hollywood.com/celebrity/1214887/Mary_Jo_Catlett|accessdate= January 22, 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Early life and education==
Catlett was born in [[Denver, Colorado]], the daughter of Cornelia M. ([[married and maiden names|née]] Callaghan) and Robert J. Catlett.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.filmreference.com/film/13/Mary-Jo-Catlett.html Mary Jo Catlett profile], FilmReference.com.com; accessed April 17, 2016.&lt;/ref&gt;
==Career==
Catlett appeared on ''[[General Hospital]]'' (for which she was nominated for a [[Daytime Emmy Award]]) and in several episodes of the television sitcom series ''[[M*A*S*H (TV series)|M*A*S*H]]''. She provided the voice of [[List of SpongeBob SquarePants characters#Mrs. Puff|Mrs. Puff]] in the animated television series ''[[SpongeBob SquarePants]]'', as well as the related film ''[[The SpongeBob SquarePants Movie]]'' (2004). In 2002, she was nominated for [[Annie Award|The Annie Award]] for Best Voice Acting by a Female Performer in an Animated Production for ''SpongeBob SquarePants''.
She also provided the voice for Cousin Larry's mother on the animated television series ''[[Kim Possible]]''. Mary Jo Cattlet has also provided voices for ''[[Rugrats]]'', ''[[The Smurfs (TV series)|The Smurfs]]'', ''[[Quack Pack]]'' and ''[[Extreme Ghostbusters]]''.
She is also known, prior to her role on ''Diff'rent Strokes'', for the commercials she did for [[Black Flag (insecticide)|Black Flag]] and its pest-control products, particularly the [[Roach Motel (insect trap)|Roach Motel]] brand.
Her many television guest roles include appearances on episodes of ''[[That's So Raven]]'', ''[[Glee (TV series)|Glee]]'' and ''[[Desperate Housewives]]''. She played wrestler &quot;Terrible Tessie&quot; in the first season ''[[Starsky &amp; Hutch]]'' episode &quot;The Omaha Tiger&quot;,&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=''Starsky &amp; Hutch'': &quot;The Omaha Tiger&quot; (1976)|url=http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0709529/fullcredits#cast|accessdate=January 22, 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; and as &quot;Cousin Alice&quot;, the slow-witted but well-meaning relation of an old acquaintance of the Duke family in the first season ''[[The Dukes of Hazzard|Dukes of Hazzard]]'' episode &quot;Swamp Molly&quot;.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=''The Dukes of Hazzard'': &quot;Swamp Molly&quot; (1979)|url=http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0567214/fullcredits#cas|accessdate=April 17, 2016}}&lt;/ref&gt; 
She had a voice role on the &quot;American Dream Factory&quot; episode of ''[[American Dad!]]''. Catlett appeared in feature films including ''[[Serial Mom]]'', ''[[The Benchwarmers]]'' and ''[[Beethoven's 5th]]''. She has appeared in an episode of ''[[2 Broke Girls]]'' with other veteran character actresses and guest starred on ''[[Shake It Up (U.S. TV series)|Shake It Up]]''.

[[File:Grand Royal.png|right|frame|Grand Royal Logo]]
'''Grand Royal ''' was the [[Los Angeles]], [[California]]-based [[Vanity label|vanity]] [[record label]] set up in [[1992 in music|1992]] by the [[Beastie Boys]] in conjunction with [[Capitol Records]] after the group left [[Def Jam Recordings]].
Due to mounting debts, Grand Royal went out of business in [[2001 in music|2001]]. Its assets were sold off via auction on [[Bid4Assets]]; these assets did not include any rights to Beastie Boys music. The assets and back catalog were purchased by a group of fans who in turn started [[GR2 Records]].
''Grand Royal'' was also the name of a magazine written and published by the group. It was most famous for its article &quot;Mulling Over the Mullet&quot; in issue two, which lampooned the [[mullet (haircut)|mullet]] hairstyle (the Beastie Boys also recorded a song called &quot;Mullet Head&quot;, which was released on Grand Royal on its ''[[Sure Shot]]'' 12-inch [[single (music)|single]]).

'''BirdLife International''' (formerly the '''International Council for Bird Preservation''')  is a global partnership of [[Conservation movement|conservation]] organisations that strives to conserve [[birds]], their [[habitat]]s and global [[biodiversity]], working with people towards sustainability in the use of natural resources. It is the world's largest partnership of conservation organisations, with over 120 partner organisations.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=BirdLife Partners|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/partnership/birdlife-partners|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==History==
BirdLife International was founded as in 1922 the ''International Council for Bird Preservation'' by American [[ornithologist]]s [[T. Gilbert Pearson]] and [[Jean Theodore Delacour]]. It changed its name in 1993 to &quot;BirdLife International&quot;.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Our History|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/partnership/our-history|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Regional work==
BirdLife International has conservation work programmes in the following parts of the world, which it describes as &quot;regions&quot; - Africa, the Americas, Asia, Europe and Central Asia, the Middle East and the Pacific.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Regions|url=http://www.birdlife.org/regions|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Africa|url=http://www.birdlife.org/africa|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Americas|url=http://www.birdlife.org/americas|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Asia|url=http://www.birdlife.org/asia|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Europe and Central Asia|url=http://www.birdlife.org/europe-and-central-asia|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Middle East|url=http://www.birdlife.org/middle-east|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Pacific|url=http://www.birdlife.org/pacific|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;
=== Member organisations ===
{{Expand section|date=August 2016}}
There are about 120 regional partners of BirdLife International, including:&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/partnership/birdlife-partners BirdLife Partners], BirdLife International (page visited on 16 August 2016).&lt;/ref&gt;
* [[National Audubon Society|Audubon Society]] (USA)
* [[BirdLife Australia]]
* [[BirdLife Cyprus]]
* [[BirdLife Hungary]]
* [[BirdLife Malta]]
* [[SOS/BirdLife Slovakia|BirdLife Slovakia]]
* [[Spanish Ornithological Society|BirdLife Spain]]
* [[BirdLife South Africa]]
* [[Environmental movement in Switzerland|Swiss Association for the Protection of Birds (BirdLife Switzerland)]]
* [[Royal Society for the Protection of Birds]]
==Global programmes==
Within each of these regions, BirdLife has nine programmes - some are well established, others are more recent and responding to specific conservation issues. In addition to the regional programmes, there are &quot;global&quot; programmes, not specific to a region. Together these programmes help the partnership to focus and work on common priorities. They provide the framework for planning, implementing, monitoring and evaluating conservation work. BirdLife programmes are; the Seabird and Marine Programme, the Birdlife Preventing Extinctions Programme, the Migratory Birds and Flyways Programme, the Climate Change Programme, the Forests of Hope Programme, the Local Empowerment Programme, the Invasive Alien Species Programme and the  [[Important Bird Area|Important Bird and Biodiversity Areas]] Programme.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Marine|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/programmes/marine|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Preventing Extinctions|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/programmes/preventing-extinctions|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Migratory Birds and Flyways|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/programmes/migratory-birds-and-flyways|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Climate Change|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/programmes/climate-change|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Forests of Hope|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/programmes/forests-hope|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Local Empowerment|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/programmes/local-empowerment|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Invasive Alien Species|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/programmes/invasive-alien-species|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Important Bird and Biodiversity Areas (IBAs)|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/programmes/important-bird-and-biodiversity-areas-ibas|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Magazine==
BirdLife International publishes a quarterly magazine, ''World Birdwatch'', which contains recent news and authoritative articles about birds, their habitats, and their conservation around the world.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=BirdLife’s World Bird Club|url=http://www.birdlife.org/worldwide/support-us/join-us#World%20Bird%20Club|publisher=BirdLife International|accessdate=30 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Red List==
BirdLife International is the official [[Red List]] authority for birds, for the [[International Union for Conservation of Nature]]. In the 2012 assessment, 1,313 species of birds are considered threatened with extinction (in the categories of [[critically endangered]], [[Endangered species|endangered]] or [[Vulnerable species|vulnerable]]). This represents 13% of the 10,064 extant bird species in the world.{{citation needed|date=August 2015}}

'''Linda Christian''' (November 13, 1923&lt;ref&gt;Ancestry.com. New Orleans Passenger Lists, 1820–1945 [database on-line]. Provo, UT, US: The Generations Network, Inc., 2006. Micropublication T905. RG085. Rolls #104. Entry for Blanca Rosa Welter Age 1yr 4 months. Gender Female. Nationality: Dutch. Port of Departure: Tampico, Mexico. Ship Name: Maasdam Port of Arrival: New Orleans, Louisiana. Date of Arrival: Mar 23, 1925.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Ancestry.com. New York Passenger Lists, 1820–1957 [database on-line]. Provo, UT, US: The Generations Network, Inc., 2006. Year: 1947; Microfilm serial: T715; Microfilm roll: T715_7515; Line: 6;. Entry for: Blanca Welter. Gender Female. Age: 24 years. Port of Departure: Southampton, England. Nationality: Mexican. Place of Birth: Tampico, Mexico. Occupation: Actress. Ship Name: Queen Mary. Port of Arrival: New York, NY. Date of Arrival: Dec 10, 1947.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Ancestry.com. New York Passenger Lists, 1820–1957 [database on-line]. Provo, UT, US: The Generations Network, Inc., 2006. Year: 1941; Microfilm serial: T715; Microfilm roll: T715_6565; Line: 9; .Entry for: Blanca Rosa Welter. Gender: Female. Age: 17. Nationality: Dutch. Occupation: Student. Place of Birth: Tampico, Mexico. Port of Departure: Cape Town, South Africa. Ship Name: President Monroe. Port of Arrival: New York, NY. Date of Arrival: July 31, 1941. Editor's note: if she was 17 in July 1941, that would mean she was going to turn 18 that November; another confirmation of 1923 as her year of birth.&lt;/ref&gt; – July 22, 2011&lt;ref name=obit&gt;{{cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2011/07/26/movies/linda-christian-actress-and-tyrone-powers-wife-dies-at-87.html?_r=1&amp;ref=deathsobituaries|title=Linda Christian, Actress and Tyrone Power's Wife, Dies at 87|work=[[Associated Press]] via [[The New York Times]]|date=July 25, 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;) was a Mexican film actress, who appeared in [[Mexican cinema|Mexican]] and [[Hollywood]] films. Her career reached its peak in the 1940s and 1950s. She played Mara in the last [[Johnny Weissmuller]] Tarzan film, ''[[Tarzan and the Mermaids]]'' (1948).&lt;ref name=parla35&gt;[[#Parla|Parla]], p. 35.&lt;/ref&gt; She is also noted for being the first [[Bond girl]], appearing in a 1954 television adaptation of the [[James Bond]] novel ''[[Casino Royale (Climax!)|Casino Royale]]''.&lt;ref&gt;Steven Jay Rubin (2003) ''The Complete James Bond Movie Encyclopedia'', Contemporary Books, pp. 70, 76–77, ISBN 0071412468.&lt;/ref&gt; In 1963 she starred in an episode of ''[[The Alfred Hitchcock Hour]]'', &quot;An Out for Oscar&quot;.
==Early life==
Christian was born as '''Blanca Rosa Welter''' in [[Tampico]], [[Tamaulipas]], [[Mexico]], a daughter of [[Dutch people|Dutch]] engineer and [[Royal Dutch Shell]] executive, Gerardus Jacob Welter (1904–1981),&lt;ref&gt;Birth and death dates given in ''Il coraggio di scrivere,'' Volume 1, by Gianni Ippoliti (Baldini Castoldi Dalai, 1992), p. 14.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Ancestry.com. New Orleans Passenger Lists, 1820–1945 [database on-line]. Provo, UT, US: The Generations Network, Inc., 2006. Port of Departure: Tampico, Mexico. Ship Name: Maasdam Port of Arrival: New Orleans, Louisiana. Date of Arrival: Mar 23, 1925.&lt;/ref&gt; and his [[Mexican people|Mexican]]-born wife, the former Blanca Rosa Vorhauer (1901-1992), who was of [[Spanish people|Spanish]], [[Germans|German]] and [[French people|French]] descent.&lt;ref&gt;Birth date given in ''Il Coraggio di Scrivere,'' Volume 1, by Gianni Ippoliti (Baldini Castoldi Dalai, 1992), p. 14.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[[#Parla|Parla]], pp. 35–36.&lt;/ref&gt; The Welter family moved a great deal during Christian's youth, living everywhere from South America and Europe to the Middle East and Africa.&lt;ref name=parla35/&gt; As a result of this nomadic lifestyle, Christian became an accomplished [[Multilingualism|polyglot]] with the ability to speak fluent French, German, Dutch, Spanish, [[English language|English]], [[Italian language|Italian]] and even a bit of haphazard [[Arabic]] and [[Russian language|Russian]].&lt;ref name=parla35/&gt;
Christian had three younger siblings, a sister, actress [[Ariadne Welter|Ariadna Gloria Welter]] (1930–1998), and two brothers, Gerardus Jacob Welter (born 1924) and Edward Albert Welter (born 1932).&lt;ref&gt;Ancestry.com. New Orleans Passenger Lists, 1820–1945 [database on-line]. Provo, UT, US: The Generations Network, Inc., 2006. Micropublication T905. RG085. Rolls #104. Port of Departure: Tampico, Mexico. Ship Name: Maasdam Port of Arrival: New Orleans, Louisiana. Date of Arrival: Mar 23, 1925.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Ancestry.com. New York Passenger Lists, 1820–1957 [database on-line]. Provo, UT, US: The Generations Network, Inc., 2006. Year: 1941; Microfilm serial: T715; Microfilm roll: T715_6565; Line: 9;. Port of Departure: Cape Town, South Africa. Ship Name: President Monroe. Port of Arrival: New York, NY.&lt;/ref&gt;
==Career==
[[File:Christian-Palma-Tarzan.jpg|right|200px|thumb|Christian and the Mexican actress [[Andrea Palma (actress)|Andrea Palma]] in ''[[Tarzan and the Mermaids]]'' in 1948.]]
In her youth Christian's only aspiration was to become a physician.&lt;ref name=parla36&gt;[[#Parla|Parla]], p. 36.&lt;/ref&gt; After she graduated from secondary school she had a fortuitous meeting with her screen idol [[Errol Flynn]], who became her lover, and she was persuaded by him to give up her hopes of joining the medical profession, move to Hollywood, and pursue an acting career.&lt;ref name=parla36/&gt; Not long after arriving in Hollywood she was spotted by [[Louis B. Mayer]]'s secretary at a fashion show in [[Beverly Hills]]. He offered, and she accepted, a seven-year contract with MGM.&lt;ref name=parla37&gt;[[#Parla|Parla]], p. 37.&lt;/ref&gt;
Her stage name was invented by Flynn, who gave her the surname of Fletcher Christian of ''Mutiny on the Bounty'' fame. Flynn had played Fletcher Christian in a 1933 Australian film.&lt;ref&gt;Thomas McNulty (2004) ''Errol Flynn: The Life and Career'', McFarland, pp. 165, 166, ISBN 0786417501.&lt;/ref&gt;
In his autobiography, ''[[My Wicked, Wicked Ways]]'',&lt;ref&gt;Errol Flynn (2002) My Wicked, Wicked Ways&quot;, Rowman &amp; Littlefield, ISBN 978-0815412502&lt;/ref&gt; Flynn states that immediately after Linda Christian's screen test, he offered to pay for her to have a couple of crooked teeth fixed. When he got a whopping bill, he discovered that she had taken the opportunity to undergo major cosmetic dentistry. Years later, when he met her again, he said, &quot;Smile, baby – I want to see those choppers: they took their first bite out of me.&quot;
She made her film debut in the 1944 musical comedy ''[[Up in Arms]]'', co-starring [[Danny Kaye]] and [[Dinah Shore]]. This movie also happened to be Danny Kaye's own first film.&lt;ref name=parla37/&gt; This film was followed by ''[[Holiday in Mexico]]'' (1946), ''[[Green Dolphin Street]]'' (1947), and what was perhaps her best-known film, ''[[Tarzan and the Mermaids]]''&lt;ref name=parla37/&gt; (1948). She was the subject of a well-known photograph published in the January 1, 1949, issue of ''[[Vogue (magazine)|Vogue]]''.
Christian was the first Bond girl to appear on screen, playing Valerie Mathis (opposite Barry Nelson as James Bond) in the 1954 TV adaptation of ''[[Casino Royale (Climax!)|Casino Royale]]'', beating [[Ursula Andress]] to the screen by eight years.
In 2001, a Golden Palm Star on the [[Palm Springs, California]], [[Palm Springs Walk of Stars|Walk of Stars]] was dedicated to her.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.palmspringswalkofstars.com/web-storage/Stars/Stars%20dedicated%20by%20date.pdf|format=PDF|title=Palm Springs Walk of Stars by date dedicated|website=palmspringswalkofstars.com}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Marriages and relationships==
[[File:LindaChristian1966Rotterdam(cropped).jpg|{{largethumb}}|Christian in Rotterdam (1966)]]
Christian's fame, however, was largely derived from having been married to (and [[divorce]]d from) the popular screen idol [[Tyrone Power]]&lt;ref name=parla35/&gt; (1949–56). The couple married in Rome, Italy, at [[Santa Francesca Romana, Rome|Santa Francesca Romana church]]; Christian wore a formfitting gold-damask gown, and the church was decorated with two thousand 'Esther' carnations.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://tyforum.bravepages.com/artc/oggi-2-3-49.html|title=Magazine Articles - Matrimonio in Technicolor|publisher=}}&lt;/ref&gt; She and Power were the parents of singer [[Romina Power]] and actress [[Taryn Power]].&lt;ref name=parla39&gt;[[#Parla|Parla]], p. 39.&lt;/ref&gt; Romina was one half of the Italian singing duo [[Al Bano and Romina Power]].
A month after she divorced Tyrone Power, Christian was seen with Spanish racing driver [[Alfonso de Portago]], who was married to American Carroll de Portago (later [[Carroll Petrie]]). Carroll had recently given birth to &quot;Fon's&quot; second child Anthony. De Portago was also dating model [[Dorian Leigh]], mother of his recently born illegitimate son Kim. Linda was photographed with de Portago at the 1957 [[Mille Miglia]] car race. The photo shows Christian leaning in to kiss Fon before he drove off and crashed his Ferrari, killing himself, his navigator Ed Nelson and at least ten spectators in the process. The press labeled the photo &quot;The Kiss of Death&quot;. De Portago was 28 years old. Her ex-husband, Tyrone Power, died the following year of a heart attack at the age of 44. Christian was later married to the [[Rome]]-based British actor [[Edmund Purdom]].&lt;ref name=obit/&gt;
On several occasions Christian and Power were offered the opportunity to work together, but for various reasons each offer was refused or rescinded.&lt;ref name=parla39/&gt; The most notable opportunity to co-star together came in 1953, when they were offered leading roles in ''[[From Here to Eternity]]''. Power did not want to do the film&lt;ref name=parla39/&gt; and rejected the offer. The roles went to [[Donna Reed]] and [[Montgomery Clift]]. 
Linda Christian died on July 22, 2011, at the age of 87.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|last =Bergan|first =Ronald|title =Linda Christian obituary|date =26 July 2011|work =The Guardian|url =https://www.theguardian.com/film/2011/jul/26/linda-christian-obituary|publisher =theguardian.com|accessdate =12 January 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The '''''Kentucky Cardinal''''' was a nightly {{convert|312|mile|adj=on}} [[passenger train]] operated by [[Amtrak]] from 1999 to 2003 between [[Chicago, Illinois]], and [[Louisville, Kentucky]], via [[Indianapolis, Indiana]]. On the three days that the ''[[Cardinal (passenger train)|Cardinal]]'' ran, the ''Kentucky Cardinal'' operated as a section, splitting at Indianapolis. On the other four days, it ran on its own to Chicago.
==History==
[[File:Hi-Level coach and express boxcar on the Kentucky Cardinal at Jeffersonville, August 2001.jpg|thumb|left|A typical ''Kentucky Cardinal'' consist: a B32-8WH locomotive (out of frame), a P42DC locomotive, a Hi-Level coach, and a boxcar]]
[[File:Kentucky Cardinal at Louisville, May 2002.jpg|thumb|left|The ''Kentucky Cardinal'' at Louisville in May 2002]]
Between May 1971–August 1974 Amtrak's ''[[Floridian (train)|Floridian]]'' ran from Chicago to [[Florida]] via Indianapolis and Louisville. Due to poor track conditions on the [[Penn Central]] trackage in [[Indiana]], the train was rerouted to the west north of [[Nashville, Tennessee]]. In April 1975 the train was routed back east over the former [[Monon Railroad]], again serving Louisville, but bypassing Indianapolis to the west. The ''Floridian'' served Louisville until its discontinuance in October 1979.&lt;ref&gt;{{Harvnb|Sanders|2006|pp=80–83}}&lt;/ref&gt;
On December 17, 1999, the ''Kentucky Cardinal'' started running as an extension of the ''[[Hoosier State (passenger train)|Hoosier State]]'', which ran four days a week between Chicago and Indianapolis (filling in the gaps in the ''Cardinal'' schedule). The ''Kentucky Cardinal'' ran as trains 850 southbound and 851 northbound. Three days of the week, it was grouped with the ''[[Cardinal (Amtrak)|Cardinal]]'', trains 50 and 51, north of Indianapolis.
The train, operated along trackage that had last been used by the ''[[Floridian (Amtrak)|Floridian]]'' in 1974, was an attempt to attract express business from [[United Parcel Service]], which maintains its air-express hub at [[Louisville International Airport]]. At first the train only ran from Indianapolis south to [[Jeffersonville, Indiana]], where a new [[Amtrak Mail and Express]] facility was built. The city of Louisville spent $370,000 in 2001 to renovate its [[Union Station (Louisville)|Union Station]] for the service, and on December 4 the ''Kentucky Cardinal'' started to use a new track on the west side of the Union Station [[parking lot]].
The train was handicapped by inconvenient departure and arrival times and slow travel times, especially on the segment from Indianapolis south to Louisville. This segment was operated over the [[Louisville and Indiana Railroad]], which had purchased the line from [[Conrail]] in 1994, and had a speed limit of 30 miles per hour (50&amp;nbsp;km/h) over most of the route due to its [[jointed rail]]. This made travel on the ''Kentucky Cardinal'' slower than [[automobile]] traffic on the parallel [[Interstate 65]].
With the opening in 1999, [[Greyhound Lines|Greyhound]] began running [[Thruway Motorcoach]] bus connections from Jeffersonville south to [[Nashville, Tennessee]], with stops at [[Elizabethtown (KY)|Elizabethtown]] and [[Bowling Green, Kentucky]]. Though rail passenger advocates tried to get the train's route extended to Nashville in an effort to increase ridership, Amtrak lost its mail contract, and the ''Kentucky Cardinal'' last ran July 4, 2003, restoring the old ''Hoosier State'' on July 6 (the ''Cardinal'' ran both ways on July 5).
==Route details==
:''For details north of [[Indianapolis (IN)|Indianapolis]], see [[Hoosier State (passenger train)#Route details|Hoosier State]].''
The tracks used were originally part of the [[Pennsylvania Railroad]] system, and are now owned by the [[Louisville and Indiana Railroad]]. The following lines were used:
*[[Louisville Bridge and Terminal Railway]] (PRR), [[Louisville, Kentucky]], to [[Clarksville, Indiana]], now L&amp;I
*[[Jeffersonville, Madison and Indianapolis Railroad]] (PRR), Clarksville to [[Indianapolis, Indiana]], now L&amp;I

      <text xml:space="preserve">{{about||people with the same or similar name|Isiah Thomas (disambiguation)}}
{{Infobox person
| name        = Isaiah Thomas
| image       = Isaiah Thomas1818.jpg
| image_size  = 255px
| alt         = Portrait of Isaiah Thomas
| caption     = Oil on canvas by [[Ethan Allen Greenwood]] in 1818 (American Antiquarian Society)
| birth_date  = {{birth date|1749|1|19}}
| birth_place = [[Boston]], [[Massachusetts]]
| death_date  = {{death date and age|1831|4|4|1749|1|30}}
| resting_place = [[Rural Cemetery (Worcester, Massachusetts)|Rural Cemetery]]&lt;br&gt;Worcester, Massachusetts
| signature   = Appletons' Thomas Isaiah signature.png
}}
'''Isaiah Thomas''' (January 19, 1749 – April 4, 1831) was an [[United States|American]] [[History of American newspapers|newspaper publisher]] and author. He performed the first public reading of the [[United States Declaration of Independence|Declaration of Independence]] in [[Worcester, Massachusetts]], and reported the first account of the [[Battles of Lexington and Concord]].  He was the founder of the [[American Antiquarian Society]].
==Biography==
===Early life and career===
Thomas was born in [[Boston]], [[Massachusetts]]. He was apprenticed on July 7, 1756 to Zechariah Fowle, a Boston printer, with whom, after working as a printer in [[City of Halifax|Halifax]], [[Portsmouth, New Hampshire|Portsmouth]] ([[New Hampshire]]) and [[Charleston, South Carolina|Charleston]] ([[South Carolina]]), he formed a partnership in 1770.&lt;ref&gt;From Colonial Times to the Present. Geraldine Youcha. New York. Scribner. 1995.&lt;/ref&gt;
===The ''Massachusetts Spy''===
[[Image:Massachusetts Spy 3a10607u.png|thumb|[[Masthead (publishing)|Masthead]] of July 7, 1774 issue]]
The partnership was formed to publish the ''[[Massachusetts Spy]]'', and lasted for three months, after which Thomas continued publication alone. For the paper's motto, he chose “Open to all parties, but influenced by none.”&lt;ref name=acab&gt;{{Appletons'|wstitle=Thomas, Isaiah|year=1900|inline=1}}&lt;/ref&gt;  Initially it came out three times each week, then (under his sole ownership) as a semi-weekly, and beginning in 1771, as a weekly. The paper soon espoused the Whig cause and was the object of government efforts to suppress it.{{sfn|Chisholm|1911}} In 1771 Gov. [[Thomas Hutchinson (governor)|Thomas Hutchinson]] ordered the attorney general to prosecute Thomas, but the grand jury failed to find cause for indictment.&lt;ref name=acab/&gt;
In Boston, in 1774, Thomas published the ''[[Royal American Magazine]]'', which was continued for a short time by [[Joseph Greenleaf]], and which contained many engravings by [[Paul Revere]].{{sfn|Chisholm|1911}}
===Escape to Worcester===
Wary of the Tories' growing resentment of the independence of the ''Spy'', on April 16, 1775 (three days before the [[Battle of Concord]], in which he took part), Thomas took his presses from Boston and set them up in [[Worcester, Massachusetts|Worcester]]. His other property was destroyed.&lt;ref name=acab/&gt;  In Worcester, he published and sold books, built a paper mill and bindery, and continued the paper until 1802 save for gaps in 1776–1778 and in 1786–1788. The ''Spy'' supported [[George Washington]] and the [[Federalist Party]]. He was also postmaster for a time.
Thomas married Mary Fowle, described as a &quot;half-cousin&quot;, on May 26, 1779.&lt;ref&gt;[[Douglas Crawford McMurtrie|McMurtrie, Douglas C.]] ''The Book: The Story of Printing &amp; Bookmaking''. New York: Oxford University Press, 1943: 431.&lt;/ref&gt; Around 1802, Thomas gave his Worcester business over to his son, including the control of the ''Spy''.{{sfn|Chisholm|1911}}
===Later life===
From 1775 until 1803, Thomas published the ''[[New England Almanac]]'', continued until 1819 by his son, Isaiah Thomas, Jr. It had something of the flavor of Benjamin Franklin's ''Poor Richard's Almanac''.&lt;ref name=acab/&gt; In 1786 he procured from Europe the first font of music type that was brought to the [[United States]], and he was the first printer in the U.S. to use such type. He was engaged at [[Walpole, New Hampshire]], in book publishing and printing the ''Farmer's Museum'', and in 1788 opened a bookstore in Boston under the firm name of Thomas and Andrews, also establishing branches of his publishing business in several parts of the United States. The monthly ''[[Massachusetts Magazine]]'' was published by the firm, with Ebenezer T. Andrews, in eight volumes, from 1789 until 1796. At Worcester he printed a folio edition of the [[Bible]] in 1791, [[Isaac Watts|Watts]]' ''Psalms and Hymns'', and most of the Bibles and school books that were used in the U.S. at that date.&lt;ref name=acab/&gt;{{sfn|Chisholm|1911}}
His ambition throughout his life was to write an extensive book on the history of publishing.
He began what would become ''[[History of Printing in America (book)|History of Printing in America]]'' in 1808.&lt;ref name=McMurtrie432&gt;[[Douglas Crawford McMurtrie|McMurtrie, Douglas C.]] ''The Book: The Story of Printing &amp; Bookmaking''. New York: Oxford University Press, 1943: 432.&lt;/ref&gt; Fully titled ''History of Printing in America, with a Biography of Printers, and an Account of Newspapers'', it was published in two volumes in 1810. A second edition, published in 1874, was prepared by his grandson Benjamin Franklin Thomas and included a catalog of American publications previous to 1776 and a memoir of Isaiah Thomas.
In November 1812, Thomas founded the American Society of Antiquaries, now known as the [[American Antiquarian Society]], partly to take care of the extensive library he had accumulated in preparing his history of publishing. At its first meeting, Thomas was elected president, a role he held until his death.&lt;ref name=McMurtrie432/&gt; In addition to nearly 8,000 volumes from his collection, he gave to its library tracts, and one of the most valuable files of newspapers in the country, and he presented land and a hall, with a provision equal to $24,000 for its maintenance.&lt;ref name=acab/&gt;
Thomas spent his final days in Worcester. Upon his death in 1831, he bequeathed his entire library, his collection of early American newspapers, as well as his personal papers and records to the American Antiquarian Society.&lt;ref name=McMurtrie432/&gt;
Thomas is now interred at [[Rural Cemetery (Worcester, Massachusetts)|Rural Cemetery]] in Worcester.&lt;ref&gt;{{FAG|5472|Isaiah Thomas, Sr}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Legacy==
Thomas's grandson [[Benjamin Thomas (congressman)|B. F. Thomas]] noted his grandfather's importance in founding the American Antiquarian Society. &quot;He saw and understood, no man better, from what infinitely varied and minute sources the history of a nation's life was to be drawn; that the only safe rule was to gather up all the fragments so that nothing be lost.&quot;&lt;ref&gt;[[Douglas Crawford McMurtrie|McMurtrie, Douglas C.]] ''The Book: The Story of Printing &amp; Bookmaking''. New York: Oxford University Press, 1943: 434.&lt;/ref&gt; In 1943, ''[[Publishers Weekly]]'' created the Carey-Thomas Award for creative publishing, naming it honor of [[Mathew Carey]] and Isaiah Thomas.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.time.com/time/magazine/article/0,9171,774310,00.html &quot;Publishers' Oscar&quot;]; ''Time'', February 15, 1943&lt;/ref&gt;
==Quotation==
{{Quote|But, to my great disappointment, I soon found that people were not to be reasoned out of measures, that they never reasoned themselves into.&lt;ref&gt;''Worcester Magazine'', v. 3, p.&amp;nbsp;46. 1787&lt;/ref&gt;}}

The insides of a typical '''[[USB flash drive|keydrive]]'''. With the plastic clamshell case removed, we can see that the keydrive consists of only two significant components. The first is the [[flash memory]] [[silicon chip|chip]] (item '''4''').  The second (item '''2''') is a device which implements the [[USB]] networking and mass-storage interface.

'''Lenka Peterson''' (born '''Betty Ann Isacson'''; October 16, 1925) is an American actress of stage, film and television.
==Life and career==
Peterson was born in [[Omaha, Nebraska]], the daughter of Lenke (née Leinweber), a lab technician, and Sven Edward Isacson, a physician.&lt;ref&gt;http://www.filmreference.com/film/59/Lenka-Peterson.html&lt;/ref&gt; One of the first members admitted to [[New York City|New York]]'s [[Actors Studio]],&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|quote=At the end of the summer, on Gadget's return from Hollywood, we settled the roster of actors for our two classes in what we called the Actors Studio - using the word 'studio' as we had when we named our workshop in the Group, the Group Theatre Studio. Kazan's people met twice a week and included, among others, Julie Harris, Jocelyn Brando, Cloris Leachman, James Whitmore, Joan Copeland, Steven Hill, Lou Gilbert, Rudy Bond, Anne Hegira, Peg Hillias, Lenka Peterson, Edward Binns, and Tom Avera.|author=Robert Lewis |title=[https://books.google.com/books?id=EVVX6pynyssC&amp;pg=PA183&amp;lpg=PA183&amp;dq=%22Joan+Copeland%22%22Actors+Studio%22&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=1a1vHo6O67&amp;sig=V9OrIQxY7qVKpEYZRVrhEdYYHUo&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=nH4yUOT0EoL30gGJu4GgAQ&amp;ved=0CEgQ6AEwAzgK#v=onepage&amp;q=%22Joan%20Copeland%22%22Actors%20Studio%22&amp;f=false Slings and Arrows: Theater in My Life] |origyear=1984|year=1996 |publisher=Applause Books |location=New York |language= |isbn= 1-55783-244-7|page=183 |chapter=Actors Studio, 1947}}&lt;/ref&gt; Peterson's [[Broadway theater|Broadway]] portrayals included Ella in ''[[Sundown Beach]]'' (1948), Maude in ''[[The Grass Harp]]'' (1952), Kitty in ''[[The Time of Your Life]]'' (1955), Sally and Mary in ''[[All the Way Home (play)|All the Way Home]]'' (1960), Rose in ''[[Nuts (play)|Nuts]]'' (1980), and Sarah in ''[[Quilters (musical)|Quilters]]'' (1984), among others.
Peterson was nominated for a 1985 [[Tony Award for Best Featured Actress in a Musical]] for her role in ''[[Quilters (musical)|Quilters]]''. She guest starred in such early television productions as ''[[Hallmark Hall of Fame]]'' (1952), ''[[The Philco Television Playhouse]]'' (1955), and ''[[Actors Studio (TV series)|Actors Studio]]'' (1949 and 1950).
She has also been a cast member of the [[television]] series ''[[Young Doctor Malone|Young Dr. Malone]]'', ''[[Search for Tomorrow]]'', playing Isabel Moore in 1962 and Evelyn Reedy in 1977, ''[[A Flame in the Wind]]'', ''[[Another World (TV series)|Another World]]'' as Marie Fenton from 1983-1984 and later Lorna Devon's adopted grandmother, and ''[[Code of Vengeance]]''.
Peterson is the mother of actress [[Glynnis O'Connor]] and [http://www.imdb.com/name/nm0640289/ Darren O'Connor] (as well as three more sons, Kevin, Brian, and Sean) by her husband Daniel O'Connor, who is retired from [[NBC]] News.

'''Prudence Anne Villiers Farrow Bruns''' (born January 20, 1948) is an American author, meditation teacher, and [[film producer]]. She is the daughter of [[film director]] [[John Farrow]] and actress [[Maureen O'Sullivan]], and the younger sister of actress [[Mia Farrow]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Pop History&quot;/&gt; She is the subject of the [[The Beatles|Beatles]] song &quot;[[Dear Prudence]]&quot;.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.pophistorydig.com/?tag=beatles-prudence-farrow|title= Dear Prudence, 1967-1968|first=Jack|last=Doyle|publisher=PopHistoryDig.com|date=July 27, 2009|accessdate=May 15, 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Early life==
Farrow was raised in the [[Catholic]] faith and attended [[convent]] schools.&lt;ref name=NFDN&gt;{{cite news|title=`Dear Prudence' living, teaching TM in Walton|work=Northwest Florida Daily News|location=Fort Walton Beach, Fla.|date= October 25, 1992|page=1C}}&lt;/ref&gt; Farrow learned the [[Transcendental Meditation technique]] (TM) in 1966 at [[UCLA]].&lt;ref name=NFDN/&gt; In 1967 Farrow became interested in [[yoga]]&lt;ref name=&quot;Girl&quot;&gt;{{cite news|title=Girl Defects From Convent|date=October 24, 1967|author=Associated Press|work=Gettysburg Times|page=5|url= https://news.google.com/newspapers?id=q2wmAAAAIBAJ&amp;sjid=lP8FAAAAIBAJ&amp;pg=902,610517&amp;dq=prudence+farrow&amp;hl=en }}&lt;/ref&gt; and opened a yoga institute at a former church in [[Boston]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news |work= The Sydney Morning Herald |date=November 9, 1967 |url= https://news.google.com/newspapers?id=RZUpAAAAIBAJ&amp;sjid=OOcDAAAAIBAJ&amp;pg=1360,2696923&amp;dq=prudence+farrow&amp;hl=en|department=Women's Section |page=8 |title= Art and fashion on show}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Girl&quot;/&gt; On January 23, 1968, Farrow, along with her sister Mia and brother John, traveled with [[Maharishi Mahesh Yogi]] from [[New York City|New York]] to [[India]],&lt;ref&gt;Gould, Jonathan (2007) Harmony Books, ''Can't Buy Me Love: The Beatles, Britain, and America'', ISBN 978-0-307-35337-5&lt;/ref&gt; and then to the Maharishi's ashram in [[Rishikesh]] for a Transcendental Meditation teacher training course.&lt;ref name=&quot;Pop History&quot;&gt;&quot;Although the Beatles left the retreat before the course on transcendental meditation was finished, Prudence Farrow, Mike Love of the Beach Boys, and others stayed on and became TM teachers&quot;&lt;/ref&gt;
[[The Beatles at Rishikesh|The Beatles arrived]] shortly thereafter, on February 16 and 20.&lt;ref&gt;The Ultimate Beatles Quiz Book, Michael Hockinson, page 128, St. Martins Press, 1990.&lt;/ref&gt; Farrow was dedicated to practicing the TM technique so she could become a TM teacher. According to Farrow: &quot;I would always rush straight back to my room after lectures and meals so I could meditate. [[John Lennon|John]], [[George Harrison|George]] and [[Paul McCartney|Paul]] would all want to sit around jamming and having a good time and I'd be flying into my room. They were all serious about what they were doing, but they just weren't as fanatical as me&quot;.&lt;ref name=&quot;Pop History&quot;/&gt; She &quot;turned into a near recluse&quot; and &quot;rarely came out&quot; of her cottage.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=NOT TUNED IN: Farrow tells story of `Dear Prudence' Sister meditated 23 hours a day, oblivious to song written for her|first=MICHAEL|last=WARREN|work=Milwaukee Journal Sentinel|date=November 5, 1995|page=8}}&lt;/ref&gt; John Lennon was asked to &quot;contact her and make sure she came out more often to socialize&quot; and he wrote the song &quot;[[Dear Prudence]]&quot;. According to Lennon,  &quot;She'd been locked in for three weeks and was trying to reach God quicker than anyone else&quot;.&lt;ref name=&quot;Pop History&quot;/&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;The Dead&quot;&gt;The American book of the Dead, Oliver Trager, page 90, Simon and Schuster, 1997&lt;/ref&gt;
==Career==
Farrow taught TM for several decades after her teacher training course in India.&lt;ref name=&quot;Pop History&quot;/&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Here, There and Everywhere: the 100 best Beatles songs, Stephen J Spignesi, Michael Lewis, page 252&lt;/ref&gt; Among those she mentored was comedian [[Andy Kaufman]].&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite book| publisher = Delacorte Press| last = Zehme| first = Bill| title = Lost in the funhouse| date = 1999-12-01|page=97}}&lt;/ref&gt; She returned to India for further instruction from the Maharishi in 1986&lt;ref name=NFDN/&gt; and has taught Transcendental Meditation in [[Florida]] since 1970.&lt;ref name=autogenerated1&gt;[http://www.tm.org/local_teachers/northeastflorida.html / NW Florida TM program web site]&lt;/ref&gt;
Later in her life, Farrow earned a BA, an MA, and a PhD from the [[University of California, Berkeley]] where she majored in Asian studies.&lt;ref name=&quot;Pop History&quot;/&gt; Her doctoral [[dissertation]] was on [[pulse diagnosis]], titled ''Nadivijnana, the Crest-Jewel of Ayurveda: A Translation of Six Central Texts and an Examination of the Sources, Influences and Development of Indian Pulse-Diagnosis''.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Seeking the Roots of Thought: Once John Lennon's muse, 'Dear Prudence' now teaches meditation|date=April 3, 2011 |first=TONY |last=SIMMONS |publisher=NewsHerald.com|location=Panama City, Florida|url=http://www.newsherald.com/articles/beach-92347-city-panama.html}}&lt;/ref&gt; Farrow became an elementary school teacher&lt;ref name=&quot;Pop History&quot;/&gt;&lt;ref name=Magee&gt;{{cite news|title=How the real 'Dear Prudence' in John Lennon's song inspired me|first=David|last=Magee|work=Chattanooga Times Free Press|date=October 17, 2009|page=B.1}}&lt;/ref&gt; and according to her résumé she has held teaching positions or presented at conferences held at the University of California at Berkeley, [[Rutgers University]] and the [[University of Wisconsin]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.prudencefbruns.com/cv.php / curriculum vitae, Prudence F. Bruns, PhD]&lt;/ref&gt;
Farrow has worked in the theater and film industry&lt;ref name=&quot;Resume&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last=Bruns|first=Prudence,|url=http://www.prudencefbruns.com/resources/Resume.pdf|title=Resume|date=October 20, 2009|accessdate=May 15, 2010|publisher=prudencefbruns.com}}&lt;/ref&gt; as a [[production assistant]] on ''[[The Muppets Take Manhattan]]'' in 1984 and the [[art department]] coordinator for ''[[The Purple Rose of Cairo]]''.&lt;ref name=&quot;Pop History&quot;/&gt; She also &quot;conceived and co-produced&quot; the 1994 film ''[[Widows' Peak]]'', which featured her sister, Mia, in a part originally written for their mother, Maureen O'Sullivan.&lt;ref name=&quot;Resume&quot;/&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.imdb.com/name/nm0268527/ Prudence Farrow - IMDB web site]&lt;/ref&gt; In 1999, she was one of four producers involved in staging ''[[Up From the Ground (play)|Up From the Ground]]'' at the Westbeth Theatre in [[New York City]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Resume&quot;/&gt; 
Farrow became a magazine writer in the 2000s.&lt;ref name=Magee/&gt; Using her married name, Prudence Bruns, she has authored articles on Asian studies, world religions, [[ayurveda]], and healthy living.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.prudencefbruns.com/publications.php / Publications by Prudence F. Bruns, PhD]&lt;/ref&gt; In 2012 Farrow established the non-profit Dear Prudence Foundation to raise funds for a [[documentary film]] of the 2013 [[Kumbh Mela]] festival which is held in India every twelve years.&lt;ref name=&quot;Destin 2013&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last=Wheeler|first=Deborah|title=For the love of TM: ‘Dear Prudence’ heading to India|url=http://www.thedestinlog.com/news/for-the-love-of-tm-dear-prudence-heading-to-india-1.77703|accessdate=January 26, 2013|newspaper=Destin Log|date=January 11, 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Personal life==
Farrow has three children and four grandchildren.&lt;ref name=&quot;Pop History&quot;/&gt; She was dating New York real estate heir [[Robert Durst]] when his wife went missing.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news| author = Charles V. Bagli and Kevin Flynn| title =A Two-Decade Spiral Into Suspicion; Long After Wife Disappears, Heir Vanishes After Texas Murder| quote = More than a decade later, Mr. Durst was dating Prudence Farrow, the subject of the Beatles song ''Dear Prudence,'' when his wife disappeared.| newspaper =[[The New York Times]]| date = October 21, 2001| url =https://www.nytimes.com/2001/10/21/nyregion/two-decade-spiral-into-suspicion-long-after-wife-disappears-heir-vanishes-after.html| accessdate =March 15, 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|date=December 4, 2000|volume=54|number=24|title=Resuming the Search|first=Bill|last=Hewitt|work=[[People (magazine)]]|url=http://www.people.com/people/archive/article/0,,20133152,00.html}}&lt;/ref&gt;

      <format>text/x-wiki</format>
      <text xml:space="preserve">[[Image:Faroese folk dance club from vagar.jpg|thumb|250px|Famous for their peaceful community, unique culture with old ballads and chain-dance, hospitality and cosmopolitanism - despite, or ''due'' to their isolated islands in the North Atlantic: Faroese people.]]
This is a list of famous or important [[Faroese people]]. In a small island-society of fewer than 50,000 inhabitants, each person can gain certain meaning for the entire nation. The people listed below, are among those who played or play a distinct role in Faroese culture, politics and history. Many of them are renowned outside the [[Faroes]], especially in Denmark and other Nordic countries.
==Artists==
===Authors and poets===
[[Image:Faroe stamp 165 hedin bru.jpg|thumb|[[Heðin Brú]]]]
[[Image:Faroe stamp 164 william heinesen.jpg|thumb|[[William Heinesen]]]]
* [[Heðin Brú]]
* [[Marianna Debes Dahl]]
* [[Joen Danielsen]]
* [[Hans Andrias Djurhuus]]
* [[Janus Djurhuus]]
* [[Jens Christian Djurhuus]]
* [[Bergtóra Hanusardóttir]]
* [[Jens Pauli Heinesen]]
* [[William Heinesen]] (also a painter, graphical artist and musician)
* [[Guðrið Helmsdal]]
* [[Rakel Helmsdal]]
* [[Ebba Hentze]]
* [[Jógvan Isaksen]]
* [[Jørgen-Frantz Jacobsen]]
* [[Steinbjørn B. Jacobsen]]
* [[Poul F. Joensen]]
* [[Oddvør Johansen]]
* [[Hanus Kamban]]
* [[Sissal Kampmann]]
* [[Marjun Syderbø Kjelnæs]]

==Explorers and travellers==
*[[Magnus Heinason]], national hero, naval hero and privateer
*[[Ove Joensen]], in 1986 journeyed alone in a rowboat from the [[Faroe Islands]] to [[Copenhagen]] in [[Denmark]], a distance of 900 nautical miles (1,700 km), the first and only person so far to do this; the journey took 41 days
*[[Grímur Kamban]], first settler
*[[Naddoddur]], explored Iceland
*[[Livar Nysted]], crossed the North Atlantic Ocean from [[New York City]] to [[Great Britain]] in a rowboat with four rowers; they broke a 114-year-old world record
*[[Tróndur Patursson]], has several times been on adventurous journeys with [[Tim Severin]] and others, first in 1976
*[[Arne Vatnhamar]], first Farose person to climb [[Mount Everest]]

'''Marika Domińczyk''' is a [[Polish American]] actress. Best known for her role as Dr. [[Eliza Minnick]] on [[Grey's Anatomy]].
==Personal life==
Domińczyk is a daughter of Aleksandra and Miroslaw &quot;Mirek&quot; Domińczyk, a leader in the [[Solidarity (Polish trade union)|Polish Solidarity movement]].&lt;ref name=&quot;filmref&quot;&gt;{{cite web | url = http://www.filmreference.com/film/17/Dagmara-Dominczyk.html |title=Dagmara Dominczyk Biography (1976-)| publisher= FilmReference.com | accessdate=July 16, 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;
She is the sister of the actress [[Dagmara Dominczyk]].&lt;ref name=filmref /&gt;
Domińczyk became engaged to actor [[Scott Foley]] in 2006.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Scott Foley to wed actress girlfriend|url=http://usatoday30.usatoday.com/life/people/2006-11-06-scott-foley_x.htm|accessdate=13 April 2016|agency=USA Today|date=6 November 2006}}&lt;/ref&gt; In June 2007, the two wed in a private ceremony in [[Hawaii]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web | url = http://www.eonline.com/gossip/kristin/detail/index.jsp?uuid=0bc4876d-dead-4b48-9a32-75d5e4efa7fe | title= It's Official: Scott Foley Is Hitched | publisher = [[E!|E! Online]] | archivedate= June 11, 2007 | archiveurl = https://web.archive.org/web/20070611092110/http://www.eonline.com/gossip/kristin/detail/index.jsp?uuid=0bc4876d-dead-4b48-9a32-75d5e4efa7fe}}&lt;/ref&gt; They have three children: daughter Malina and sons Keller and Konrad (b. November 13, 2014).&lt;ref name=tvg-expecting&gt;{{cite news| url=http://www.usmagazine.com/celebrity-moms/news/scott-foleys-wife-gives-birth-couple-welcomes-baby-boy-konrad-foley-20141911 | title= Scott Foley's Wife Marika Dominczyk Gives Birth, Couple Welcomes Baby Boy Konrad Foley| date= November 19, 2014 |accessdate= November 3, 2016| first=Stephanie |last=Webber | work= [[Us Weekly]]}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;!--per cited source, Us Weekly, Konrad birth confirmed by parents' representative: &quot;Scandal star Scott Foley and his wife, actress Marika Dominczyk, welcomed a baby boy, Konrad Foley, on November 13th,&quot; Foley's rep tells Us in a statement. &quot;He joins big sister Malina, brother Keller and their dog Frankie. The Foley family is overjoyed.&quot;. Rep did specificallyconfirm birth dates for other two children--&gt;
==Career==
In 2004, Domińczyk appeared in the short-lived television series ''[[The Help (TV series)|The Help]]'', which also starred [[Mindy Cohn]] and [[Antonio Sabàto, Jr.]] The following year, she appeared as Bernadette in the 2005 movie ''[[The 40-Year-Old Virgin]]''. From 2006-2007, she guest-starred on the [[American Broadcasting Company|ABC]]'s ''[[Brothers &amp; Sisters (2006 TV series)|Brothers &amp; Sisters]]'' as Tyler Altamirano. She reprised her role in the last two episodes of the show's fifth season. Despite being Polish, her looks have often led to her being cast in [[Hispanic]] roles, such as Anna in ''The Help'' and, most recently, as South American agent Isabelle in ''[[Get Smart's Bruce and Lloyd: Out of Control]]''. She appeared in ''[[Who Do You Love? (2008 film)|Who Do You Love?]]'', ''[[Las Vegas (TV series)|Las Vegas']]'' &quot;3 Babes, 100 Guns, and a Fat Chick&quot; (2008) as a bounty hunter, and Danny's Marine Sergeant in Iraq, and as a Russian informant to Cool Breeze, played by her husband, in ''[[The Unit]]''. She also is in ''[[National Lampoon's Bag Boy]]''.
Domińczyk played the role of Lara in the film ''[[I Hope They Serve Beer in Hell (film)|I Hope They Serve Beer in Hell]]''. She is recently guest-starring in [[American Broadcasting Company|ABC]]'s drama series ''[[Grey's Anatomy]]'' as Dr. [[Eliza Minnick]], an ortho surgeon and a love interest for Dr [[Arizona Robbins]]

{{Taxobox
| name = Yellow mongoose
| status = LC | status_system = IUCN3.1
| status_ref = &lt;ref name=iucn&gt;{{IUCN2014.2|assessor=Taylor, P.J.|assessor2=Hoffmann, M.|last-assessor-amp=yes |year=2008|id=41597|title=Cynictis penicillata|downloaded=29 August 2014}} Database entry includes a brief justification of why this species is of least concern&lt;/ref&gt;
| image = Cynictis penicillata (Etosha, 2011).jpg
| regnum = [[Animal]]ia
| phylum = [[Chordate|Chordata]]
| classis = [[Mammal]]ia
| ordo = [[Carnivora]]

| subfamilia = [[Herpestinae]]
| genus = '''''Cynictis'''''
| genus_authority = [[William Ogilby|Ogilby]], 1833
| species = '''''C. penicillata'''''
| binomial = ''Cynictis penicillata''
| binomial_authority = [[Georges Cuvier|Cuvier]], 1829
| range_map = Yellow Mongoose area.png
| range_map_caption = Yellow mongoose range

The '''yellow mongoose''' (''Cynictis penicillata''), sometimes referred to as the '''red meerkat''', is a member of the [[mongoose]] family averaging about 1&amp;nbsp;lb (1/2&amp;nbsp;kg) in weight and about 20 in (500&amp;nbsp;mm) in length.&lt;ref name = MSW3&gt;{{MSW3 Carnivora | id = 14000508 | page = 564}}&lt;/ref&gt; It lives in open country, from [[semi-desert]] [[scrubland]] to [[grasslands]] in [[Angola]], [[Botswana]], [[South Africa]], [[Namibia]], and [[Zimbabwe]]. 
[[File:Yellow Mongoose Klipriviersberg Johannesburg.jpg|thumbnail|Yellow mongoose, Klipriviersberg, Johannesburg]]
==Taxonomy==
''Cynictis penicillata'' is the [[Monotypic taxon|only member]] of its [[genus]], but as many as twelve [[subspecies]] of yellow mongoose have been described. In general, the yellow mongoose has lighter highlights on the underbelly and chin, a bushy tail, and a complete lack of [[sexual dimorphism]]. Southern yellow mongooses are larger, have yellow or reddish fur, longer fur, and a longer [[tail]] with a characteristic white tip. Northern subspecies tend towards smaller size, grey colouration, a grey or darker grey tip to the tail, and shorter hair more appropriate to the hotter climate.
==Habits==
[[File:Fuchsmanguste 2.jpg|thumb|upright|left|Yellow mongoose]]
The yellow mongoose is [[carnivore|carnivorous]], consuming mostly [[arthropod]]s but also other small mammals, [[lizard]]s, [[snake]]s and [[egg (biology)|eggs]] of all kinds.
The yellow mongoose is primarily [[diurnal animal|diurnal]], though [[nocturnal]] activity has been observed. Living in colonies of up to 20 individuals in a permanent underground [[burrow]] complex, the yellow mongoose will often co-exist with [[Cape ground squirrel]]s or [[suricate]]s and share maintenance of the warren, adding new tunnels and burrows as necessary. The tunnel system has many entrances, nearby which the yellow mongoose makes its [[latrine (animal)|latrine]]s.
==Social structure==
[[File:Cynictis penicillata, Lake District Wildlife Park.jpg|thumb|A yellow mongoose in Lake District Wildlife Park, Cumbria, northwestern England.]]
The [[Presociality|social structure]] of the yellow mongoose is [[hierarchy|hierarchical]], based around a central breeding pair and their most recent offspring. There are also subadults, the elderly, or adult relatives of the central pair. Male ranges tend to overlap, while females from other dens have contiguous non-overlapping ranges. Every day, the alpha male will mark members of his group with anal gland secretions, and his boundaries with facial and anal secretions, as well as urine. The alpha male also rubs his back against raised objects, leaving behind hair as a visual [[territorial marking|marker of territory]]. Other members of the group mark their dens with cheek secretions. A colony can have 20-40 members.
==Predators==
[[Predator]]s of the yellow mongoose are [[birds of prey]], [[snake]]s and [[jackal]]s. When frightened, the yellow mongoose will growl and secrete from its anal glands. It can also scream, bark, and purr, though these are exceptions, as the yellow mongoose is usually silent, and communicates mood and status through tail movements.
==Mating season==
[[File:Cynictis_penicillata_mating2.jpg|thumb|Yellow mongooses mating]]
The yellow mongoose's mating season is between July and September, and it gives birth underground between October and December, with no bedding material, in a clean chamber of the [[burrow]] system. Usually, two offspring are produced per pregnancy, and they are [[wean]]ed at 10 weeks, reaching adult size after 10 months.
==Rabies==
There is some concern about the role of ''Cynictis penicillata'' as a [[natural reservoir]] of [[rabies]]. Most African wild animals will die within several weeks of infection with rabies, but it seems that certain genetic strains of the yellow mongoose can carry it asymptomatically, but infectiously, for years.&lt;ref name=&quot;pmid7777324&quot;&gt;{{cite journal |author=Taylor PJ |title=A systematic and population genetic approach to the rabies problem in the yellow mongoose (Cynictis penicillata) |journal=Onderstepoort J. Vet. Res. |volume=60 |issue=4 |pages=379–87 |date=December 1993 |pmid=7777324 |doi= |url=}}&lt;/ref&gt;

*O.A.E. [[Anne Rasa|Rasa]]; B.A. Wenhold; P. Howard; A. Marais: &quot;Reproduction in the yellow mongoose revisited&quot; in ''South African Journal of Zoology'' (1992), Vol. 27, No. 4, p.&amp;nbsp;192.
*B.A. Wenhold; O.A.E. Rasa: &quot;Territorial marking in the Yellow mongoose Cynictis penicillata: sexual advertisement for subordinates?&quot; in ''Zeitschrift für Säugetierkunde'' (1994), Vol.59, No.3, p.&amp;nbsp;129.

'''''Airborne Ranger''''' is a [[video game]] developed and released by [[MicroProse]] in 1987 for the [[Amstrad CPC]], [[Commodore 64]], [[ZX Spectrum]], and [[MS-DOS]].  It is an [[action game]] in which a sole [[United States Army Rangers|U.S. Army Ranger]] is sent to infiltrate the enemy territory to complete various objectives. [[Amiga]] and [[Atari ST]] versions were released few years later and were a wholly independent development effort and different from gameplay standpoint.{{cn|date=December 2016}}
The game was followed by ''[[Special Forces (video game)|Special Forces]]'' in 1991.
==Gameplay==
The game consists of several missions, in which the player controls a sole Ranger whose objectives include killing an enemy officer, destroying an enemy [[bunker]], taking out a [[Surface-to-air missile|SAM]] site, and rescuing a captured POW, which would possibly free a roster member that was labeled P.O.W. The game creates the maps and objective locations randomly, so the player is required to plan each mission carefully, because no mission is the same.  
At the start of each mission, the player is presented with a short overview of the mission, and can select a Ranger from a roster of available soldiers. The player is then in control of an aircraft, described as a [[Bell-Boeing V-22 Osprey|V-22 Osprey]] and is allowed to drop three [[ammunition|ammo]] crates over the enemy territory. Once the three containers are dropped, the Ranger is [[parachute]]d into the area. Upon touch-down, the player has to overcome several obstacles, including enemy soldiers and officers, [[land mine|mine fields]], [[Defensive fighting position|foxholes]] and bunkers. Due to limited ammunition, the player needs to plan his path through the territory. The dropped ammo crates provide the soldier with fresh [[hand grenade]]s and ammo. After completing the mission, the Ranger has to navigate to a pick-up point within a time limit. If the Ranger is captured (but not killed), the player can start an optional rescue mission using another soldier from the roster. Each successful mission increases the rank of the individual Ranger, up to [[colonel]].
==Manual==
The manual mimics a Field Manual&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.globalsecurity.org/military/library/policy/army/fm/ US Field Manual Index]&lt;/ref&gt;  of the U.S. Army, and brings the number of code FM75-041, non-existent outside the fiction of the game. The manual includes 40 pages in black and white, plus the color cover of thin cardboard. It is divided into two parts with 11 chapters in all. In the second part talks about the true story of U.S.  Rangers from 1759 to the [[American Revolution]] to [[World War II]], the [[Korean War]], until today, with the [[Vietnam War]], the [[invasion of Grenada]] in 1983, their training and equipment, operations  mountains, desert and jungle, consisting of a paraglider, a Colt [[CAR-15]] rifle, on the hand fragmentation [[M26 grenade]], a grenade launcher [[M72 LAW]], the [[M18 Claymore anti-personnel mine]] and the ranger knife F7.  Aside from informational purposes, the manual was used as a form of copyright protection - each time you loaded the game it would give the player a multiple choice question on the identification of military ribbons, which were listed at the top of each page throughout the booklet.  If the player answered correctly, the game loaded.  If guessed incorrectly, the game wouldn't load.
==Reception==
A review in ''[[Computer Gaming World]]'' felt ''Airborne Ranger'' was reminiscent of the earlier arcade game ''[[Commando (arcade game)|Commando]]'', but much deeper and more versatile. The graphics and sound were praised, noting gunfire sounds different when shot from inside fortifications than it does outside fortifications.&lt;ref name=&quot;CGW&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|date=January 1988|last=Rohrer|first=Kevin|periodical=[[Computer Gaming World]]|title=Airborne Ranger|pages=18|postscript =}}&lt;/ref&gt;
A 1992 survey of computer wargames with modern settings gave the game four and a half stars out of five.&lt;ref name=&quot;brooks199206&quot;&gt;{{cite news | url=http://www.cgwmuseum.org/galleries/index.php?year=1992&amp;pub=2&amp;id=95 | title=The Modern Games: 1950 - 2000 | work=Computer Gaming World | date=June 1992 | accessdate=24 November 2013 | author=Brooks, M. Evan | pages=120}}&lt;/ref&gt; It also received 4½ out of 5 stars in ''[[Dragon (magazine)|Dragon]]''.&lt;ref name=&quot;Dragon135&quot;&gt;{{cite journal|title=The Role of Computers|last1=Lesser|last2=Lesser|last3=Lesser|first1=Hartley|first2=Patricia|first3=Kirk|journal=Dragon|issue=135|date=July 1988|pages=82–89}}&lt;/ref&gt;
''[[Compute!'s Gazette]]'' noted that ''Airborne Ranger'' was an unusual game for MicroProse's developers given their history of publishing simulations, writing &quot;they have created an arcade game, and a darned good one&quot;.&lt;ref name=&quot;bobo198805&quot;&gt;{{cite news | url=https://archive.org/stream/1988-05-computegazette/Compute_Gazette_Issue_59_1988_May#page/n31/mode/2up | title=Airborne Ranger | work=Compute's Gazette | date=May 1988 | accessdate=6 October 2013 | author=Bobo, Ervin | pages=31-32}}&lt;/ref&gt; ''[[Compute!]]'' stated that &quot;''Airborne Ranger'' is an excellent game from beginning to end&quot;, but cautioned that &quot;the violence and action are graphic and highly realistic&quot;.&lt;ref name=&quot;bixby198805&quot;&gt;{{cite news | url=https://archive.org/stream/1988-05-compute-magazine/Compute_Issue_096_1988_May#page/n65/mode/2up | title=Airborne Ranger | work=Compute! | date=May 1988 | accessdate=10 November 2013 | author=Bixby, Robert | pages=65}}&lt;/ref&gt;
