[[File:MISSIONNAIRES MORMONS.JPG|thumb|right|[[Missionary (LDS Church)#Companionships|Two missionaries]] of The Church of Jesus-Christ of Latter-day Saints]]
'''Missionaries of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints''' (LDS Church)—widely known as '''Mormon missionaries'''—are volunteer representatives of the LDS Church who engage variously in [[proselytizing]], church service, [[humanitarian aid]], and community service. Mormon missionaries may serve on a full- or part-time basis, depending on the assignment, and are organized geographically into [[Mission (LDS Church)|missions]]. The mission assignment could be to any one of the [[List of missions of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints|418]]&lt;ref name=&quot;Statistics 2015&quot;&gt;[http://www.mormonnewsroom.org/article/2015-statistical-report-april-2016-general-conference &quot;2015 Statistical Report for 2016 April General Conference&quot;], ''Mormon Newsroom'', April 2, 2016.&lt;/ref&gt; missions organized worldwide.
The LDS Church is one of the most active modern practitioners of [[missionary]] work, reporting that it had over 74,000 full-time missionaries worldwide at the end of 2015&lt;ref name=&quot;Statistics 2015&quot; /&gt; (as well as nearly 32,000 part-time church-service missionaries).&lt;ref name=&quot;Statistics 2015&quot; /&gt;  Most full-time Mormon missionaries are single young men and women in their late teens and early twenties and older couples no longer with children in the home. Missionaries are often assigned to serve far from their homes, including in other countries. Many missionaries learn a new language at a [[missionary training center]] as part of their assignment. Missions typically last two years for males, 18 months for females, and 6 to 18 months for older couples. The LDS Church strongly encourages, but does not require, missionary service for young men. All Mormon missionaries serve voluntarily and do not receive a salary for their work; they typically finance missions themselves or with assistance from family or other church members. Many Latter-day Saints save money during their teenage years to cover their mission expenses.
Throughout the [[History of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints|church's history]], over one million missionaries have been sent on missions.&lt;ref name = onemillion1&gt;{{citation |url= http://www.mormonnewsroom.org/article/one-million-missionaries-thirteen-million-members |title= News Story {{ndash}} One Million Missionaries, Thirteen Million Members |work= Newsroom |publisher= LDS Church |date= June 25, 2007 |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name = onemillion2&gt;{{citation |url= http://www.deseretnews.com/article/680194052/1-million-missionaries-for-LDS-Church--so-far.html |title= 1 million missionaries for LDS Church – so far |last= Walch |first= Tad |newspaper= Deseret Morning News |date= June 26, 2007 |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Preparation to serve==
===Significance and basic qualifications===
LDS Church [[President of the Church (LDS Church)|president]] [[Spencer W. Kimball]] said, &quot;Every young man should fill a mission&quot;.&lt;ref name=kimball&gt;{{citation |url= http://www.lds.org/ensign/1974/10/when-the-world-will-be-converted?lang=eng |title= When the World Will Be Converted |last= Kimball |first= Spencer W. |authorlink= Spencer W. Kimball |journal= [[Ensign (LDS magazine)|Ensign]] |date= October 3, 1974 |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}&lt;/ref&gt; Completing a mission is often described as a [[rite of passage]] for a young Latter-day Saint.&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |first= Gary |last= Shepherd |first2= Gordon |last2= Shepherd |year= 1998 |title= Mormon Passage: A Missionary Chronicle |location= Urbana |publisher= University of Illinois Press |isbn= 0-252-06662-6 |pages= 19–34}}.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name= metamorphosis&gt;{{citation |authorlink= Peggy Fletcher Stack |first= Peggy Fletcher |last= Stack |title= Mission metamorphosis |newspaper= [[Salt Lake Tribune]] |date= June 30, 2007 |url= http://www.sltrib.com/ci_6262037 |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |first= David D. |last= Kirkpatrick |url= https://www.nytimes.com/2007/11/15/us/politics/15romney.html?pagewanted=all |title= Romney, Searching and Earnest, Set His Path in '60s |newspaper= [[The New York Times]] |date= November 15, 2007 |accessdate= 2012-11-27 |archiveurl= https://web.archive.org/web/20090425165207/http://www.nytimes.com/2007/11/15/us/politics/15romney.html?pagewanted=all |archivedate= April 25, 2009 |deadurl=no }}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book |first= Keith |last= Parry |contribution= The Mormon Missionary Companionship |page= 182 |year= 2001 |origyear= 1994 |title= Contemporary Mormonism: Social Science Perspectives |editor1-first= Marie |editor1-last= Cornwall |editor1-link= Marie Cornwall |editor2-first= Tim B. |editor2-last= Heaton |editor3-first= Lawrence A. |editor3-last= Young |place= Urbana |publisher= University of Illinois Press |isbn= 0-252-02076-6 |oclc= 28721262 }}&lt;/ref&gt; The phrase &quot;the best two years of my life&quot; is a common cliché among returned missionaries when describing their experience.&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |last= Durham |first= Lowell M., Jr. |url= http://www.lds.org/new-era/1973/06/return-of-the-missionary?lang=eng |title= Return of the Missionary |journal= [[New Era (magazine)|New Era]] |date=June 1973 |page= 54 |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |first= Janet |last= Brigham |url= http://www.lds.org/ensign/1978/12/when-the-best-two-years-are-over?lang=eng |title= When 'The Best Two Years' Are Over |journal= [[Ensign (LDS magazine)|Ensign]] |date=December 1978 |page= 29 |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}.&lt;/ref&gt; Although [[Gordon B. Hinckley]] had suggested that a mission is not to be a rite of passage,&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |url= http://www.lds.org/ensign/1990/09/news-of-the-church?lang=eng |title= News of the Church {{ndash}} New Mission Presidents Counseled to Love Their Missionaries and the People |journal= [[Ensign (LDS magazine)|Ensign]] |date=September 1990 |pages= 74–75 |first= LaRene |last= Gaunt |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}.&lt;/ref&gt; this cultural aspect remains.{{citation needed|date=December 2011}} With the usual starting age of 18–20, a mission provides a clear event or marker for the traditional age of adulthood, but is not necessary for continuance in church membership.
Young men between the ages of 18 and 25 who meet standards of worthiness are strongly encouraged to consider a two-year, full-time [[Proselytism|proselytizing]] mission. This expectation is based in part on the [[New Testament]] passage &quot;Go ye therefore, and teach all nations&quot; ([[s:Bible (King James)/Matthew#Chapter 28|Matt. 28: 19–20]]). The minimum age had previously been age 19 in most countries until October 6, 2012, when church president [[Thomas S. Monson]] announced that all male missionaries, regardless of nation, could serve from age 18.&lt;ref name= missionaryAge&gt;{{citation |title= News Release |work= Newsroom {{ndash}} Church Lowers Age Requirement for Missionary Service |url= http://www.mormonnewsroom.org/article/church-lowers-age-requirement-for-missionary-service |date= October 6, 2012 |publisher= LDS Church |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}&lt;/ref&gt;  Prior to the announcement, some countries held that male missionaries may be 18 years old because of educational or military requirements.&lt;ref name= youngmissionaries&gt;{{citation |last= Stack |first= Peggy Fletcher |title= Some Mormon men can go on missions at age 18 |url= http://www.sltrib.com/sltrib/blogsfaithblog/52458313-180/mormon-mission-age-academy.html.csp |newspaper= [[The Salt Lake Tribune]] |date= August 25, 2011 |authorlink= Peggy Fletcher Stack |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}&lt;/ref&gt;  It was also announced that young women may serve beginning at age 19 instead of 21.&lt;ref name = missionaryAge/&gt;  In 2007, approximately 30% of all 19-year-old LDS men became Mormon missionaries; from LDS families that are active in the church, approximately 80–90% of 19-year-old men serve a mission.&lt;ref name= metamorphosis/&gt;
In cases where an immediate family member dies, the missionary has the choice to travel home for the funeral or to remain on the mission. {{citation needed|date=October 2014}} Missionaries can be sent home for violating mission rules, and occasionally missionaries choose to go home for health or various other reasons. However, the vast majority of missionaries serve the whole two-year (men) or eighteen-month (women) terms.{{citation needed|date=October 2014}}
As of 2007, 80% of all Mormon missionaries were young, single men, 13% were young single women and 7% retired couples.&lt;ref name = metamorphosis/&gt; Women who would like to serve a mission must meet the same standards of worthiness and be at least 19 years old. Women generally serve as missionaries for 18 months. Married retired couples, on the other hand, are encouraged to serve missions, but their length of service may vary from 6 to 36 months depending on their circumstances and means.&lt;ref name= chasehall&gt;{{citation |first= Chase |last= Hall |url= http://www.sltrib.com/sltrib/lifestyle/51975755-80/church-missionaries-senior-lds.html.csp |title= Shorter stints may draw more senior LDS missionaries |newspaper= [[Salt Lake Tribune]] |date= 2011-06-15 |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}.&lt;/ref&gt; Any single retired person may also be called to serve in what is known as ''senior missionary service''. In the last two decades, the LDS Church has stepped up its call for senior couple missionaries.
===Standards of worthiness===
All missionaries must meet certain minimum standards of worthiness. Among the standards that a prospective missionary must demonstrate adherence to are: regular attendance at [[Worship services of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints|church meetings]], regular personal [[prayer]], regular study of the [[Standard Works|scriptures]], adherence to the [[law of chastity]] (sexual purity), adherence to the [[Word of Wisdom]] (code of health and nutrition), payment of [[tithe|tithing]], spiritual diligence and testimony of God.
===Other exclusionary factors===
In addition to spiritual preparedness, church [[Bishop (Latter Day Saints)|bishops]] are instructed to ensure that prospective missionaries are physically, mentally, and emotionally capable of full-time missionary work. In the same speech where he called for &quot;every young man&quot; to fill a mission, Kimball added, &quot;we realize that while all men definitely should, all men are not prepared to teach the gospel abroad.&quot;&lt;ref name=kimball /&gt; Apart from general issues of worthiness and ability, there are a number of specific situations that will disqualify a person from becoming a full-time missionary for the LDS Church. Those excluded include those who would have to leave dependent children in the care of someone else; young couples who are still of childbearing age; those who are in debt and have not made arrangements to meet these obligations; those who are on legal probation or parole; couples with serious unresolved marital problems; those who are HIV-positive; and those who have been convicted of sexual abuse. Additionally, members who have submitted to, performed, encouraged, paid for, or arranged for an abortion (except in the case of rape, incest, or the mothers life is in danger) are usually excluded from missionary service, as are members who have fathered or borne a child out of wedlock; men under 26 and women under 40 who have been divorced; and anyone who has participated in homosexual activity after age 16.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book |title= [[Church Handbook of Instructions]], Book 1: Stake Presidencies and Bishoprics |location= Salt Lake City |publisher= LDS Church |year= 2006 |pages= 92–4 }}&lt;/ref&gt;
From the beginnings of the LDS Church, people of black African descent could be members of the church, but up until 1978 the LDS Church did not call men of black African descent to serve on missions, due to the [[Black people and Mormonism|ban on blacks holding the priesthood]]. The priesthood ban [[Official Declaration—2|was lifted]] during [[Spencer W. Kimball|Kimball's]] presidency and since 1978 there has been no restrictions to missionary service that are based on race or ethnicity.&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |url= http://www.lds.org/topics/priesthood-ordination-before-1978?lang=eng |title= Study By Topic: Gospel Topics {{ndash}} Priesthood Ordination before 1978 |work= LDS.org |publisher= LDS Church |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}&lt;/ref&gt;
===Mission call===
After application to the church and the requisite approval, prospective missionaries receive a &quot;call to serve&quot;—an official notification of their location assignment—through the mail from the [[President of the Church (LDS Church)|President of the Church]]. The mission call also informs the prospective missionary what language he or she will be expected to use during his or her mission. Members of the [[Quorum of the Twelve Apostles (LDS Church)|Quorum of the Twelve Apostles]] are responsible for assigning missionaries to a particular mission. A prospective missionary receiving the call to serve is generally considered to be a major event in LDS culture; family members, friends, and members of the prospective missionary's congregation often gather together when a call arrives, which the prospective missionary opens and reads aloud to the group.
===Temple attendance===
Before beginning their mission, prospective male missionaries are usually [[ordination|ordained]] to the office of an [[Elder (Mormonism)|elder]] in the [[Melchizedek priesthood (Latter Day Saints)|Melchizedek Priesthood]] (if they do not hold this office already). All missionaries are [[setting apart|set apart]] by the [[laying on of hands]] to preach the gospel; this is usually performed by the missionary's [[stake president]]. Prospective missionaries also usually attend the [[Temple (LDS Church)|temple]] for the first time to receive their [[Endowment (Latter Day Saints)|endowment]] if they have not already done so.
===Training===
[[File:Missionary training center provo ut.jpg|thumb|300px|The [[Missionary Training Center]] in [[Provo, Utah|Provo]], [[Utah]], [[United States]], is one of 15 such centers located throughout the world.]]
[[File:MTC entrance.JPG|thumb|right|The Provo MTC is the LDS Church's largest [[Missionary Training Center]].]]
Newly called missionaries attend a short training period at one of 15 church [[Missionary Training Center]]s (MTCs) worldwide.&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |first= Scott |last= Taylor |date= March 23, 2011 |title= The 14 international Missionary Training Centers (MTCs) |url= http://www.deseretnews.com/top/131/0/The-14-international-Missionary-Training-Centers-MTCs.html |newspaper= [[Deseret News]] }}&lt;/ref&gt; The largest MTC is located in [[Provo, Utah]],&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |url= http://www.mtc.byu.edu/themtc.htm |title= Missionary Training Center, Provo Utah {{ndash}} About the MTC |work= mtc.byu.edu |publisher= [[Brigham Young University]] |accessdate= 2012-11-27 }}&lt;/ref&gt; adjacent to [[Brigham Young University]]. Missionaries who will not be learning a language in order to serve their missions spend three weeks at an MTC where they practice using proselytizing materials, learn expected conduct, and study the [[Standard Works|scriptures]]. Missionaries bound for foreign-language missions spend six to nine weeks at an MTC, depending on the language to be learned. During this period, they are encouraged not to speak in their native tongue but rather to immerse themselves in the new language.
==Missionary conduct==
===The Missionary Handbook===
The basic standards of missionary service and conduct are contained in the ''Missionary Handbook''.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;&gt;{{citation |title= Missionary Handbook |url= https://missionary.lds.org/missionary/Missionary%20Handbook.pdf |publisher= LDS Church |year= 2006 |accessdate= 2012-11-27}}&lt;/ref&gt; Missionaries are instructed that following these standards will protect them both physically and spiritually. Mission presidents have discretion to adjust some of the standards according to local circumstances.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|1}} The ''Missionary Handbook'' is also commonly and informally referred to as &quot;the white handbook&quot;.&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |url= http://www.sltrib.com/sltrib/news/52648912-78/missionaries-church-lds-missionary.html.csp?page=2 |title= Mormon missions: Did you know ... ? |newspaper= [[The Salt Lake Tribune]] |date= September 30, 2011 |accessdate= 2012-11-27}}.&lt;/ref&gt;
===Dress and grooming===
Previously, full-time LDS missionaries were required to adhere to a dress code: for men, conservative, dark trousers and suit coats, white dress shirts, and conservative ties. For women, modest and professional dresses or blouses and mid-calf length skirts were worn.
In recent years,{{When|date=December 2013}} the LDS Church has updated their grooming standards. Young men are no longer required to wear dark suits, and they do not have to wear a full suit during regular everyday proselyting activities. They must, however, remain in professional, conservative attire. For instance, a light colored suit is acceptable. They are also allowed to wear a sweater or suit vest over their dress shirt and are encouraged to wear colored ties.&lt;ref&gt;[http://missionary.lds.org/dress-grooming/elder/guidelines/ &quot;Missionary: Dress and Grooming: Elders: General Guidelines&quot;], lds.org.&lt;/ref&gt;
Sister missionaries may wear skirts and dresses that cover their knees. In areas infested with mosquitoes, particularly in [[Central America|Central]] and [[South America]] where the humidity yields infestations, sister missionaries are now permitted to wear slacks.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.mormonnewsroom.org/article/missionary-dress-guidelines-revised-help-prevent-mosquito-borne-diseases Missionary Dress Guidelines Revised to Help Prevent Mosquito-Borne Diseases], ''Mormon Newsroom'', 20 May 2016. Retrieved 20 May 2016.&lt;/ref&gt; Young women are encouraged to dress in colors and patterns, and they may wear appropriate jewelry and accessories.&lt;ref&gt;[http://missionary.lds.org/dress-grooming/sister/guidelines/ &quot;Missionary: Dress and Grooming: Sisters: General Guidelines&quot;], lds.org.&lt;/ref&gt;
[[File:Mormon missionaries at Teotihuacan.jpg|left|thumb|Missionaries in Mexico, dressed for tropical weather|alt=Two young men, the one on the left darker-skinned than the one on the right, who is drinking some orange liquid from a plastic bottle, standing on a stone surface with mountains in the background. They are both carrying backpacks and wearing white dress shirts, ties and slacks. The one on the right is wearing a short-sleeved shirt and tan slacks]]
In some areas these standards are altered slightly according to the discretion of the [[mission president]]. For example, in hot, humid climates, suit coats are not required, and dress shirts may be short-sleeved. Casual clothes may be worn only in limited circumstances, such as when missionaries provide manual labor or exercise.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Missionary Handbook|url=http://missionary.lds.org/missionary/Missionary%20Handbook.pdf|publisher=LDS Church|accessdate=5 June 2014|page=19}}&lt;/ref&gt;
In 2016, the dress guidelines were updated to allow for &quot;simple and conservative&quot; sunglasses and &quot;wide-brimmed hats&quot; as part of a missionary's attire to provide missionaries protection from excessive heat.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.deseretnews.com/article/865654548/Updated-LDS-missionary-dress-and-grooming-guidelines-allow-for-sunglasses.html &quot;Updated LDS Missionary dress and grooming guidelines allow for sunglasses&quot;], ''[[Deseret News]]'', 18 May 2016. Retrieved 19 May 2016.&lt;/ref&gt;
All full-time missionaries wear a name tag that gives their surname with the appropriate title (&quot;Elder&quot; or &quot;Sister&quot; in English-speaking areas, or their equivalent titles in other languages). The name tag also bears the church's name, unless the mission president considers this inadvisable due to circumstances in the area (e.g., adverse political conditions). Missionaries are required to wear the tag at all times in public.{{Update|date=December 2013}}
===Companionships===
A ''missionary companionship'', consisting of two (or occasionally, three) missionaries, is the smallest organizational unit of a mission. Every missionary is assigned by the [[mission president]] to be another missionary's ''companion''. Missionary companionships are generally maintained for months at a time and most missionaries will have served with multiple companions by the end of their mission. These companions very rarely have prior acquaintance outside of the mission. Companionships are always of the same gender.
Missionary companions are instructed to stay together at all times and not to go out of the hearing of their companion's voice.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|30–31}} Privacy is allowed only for personal care such as showering. One of the intentions of this strict policy of staying together is to discourage missionaries from breaking any mission rules.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|31}} Companions share the same living quarters and the same bedroom, but not the same bed.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|31}}
When companions have conflicting personalities or interests, they are encouraged to try to resolve them themselves. If a missionary's companion is having difficulty with the work or with personal problems missionaries are instructed to give criticism constructively, in private and with respect.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|30}} In dealing with a problem missionaries are first to raise the issue with their companion and if it is not resolved to raise it with the mission president. &quot;A missionary's first priority is to the Lord, then to the mission president and finally to their companion&quot;, as the missionary handbook states.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|32}} High value is placed on the spiritual commitment to the virtues of humility and love. Missionaries are urged to treat the companionship as a relationship that must succeed in being cooperative and selfless, thus improving the spirituality, character and social skills of each individual missionary.
===Senior couples===
Senior couples serve as a companionship for the entirety of their mission and have more relaxed rules. Unlike single missionaries, they share the same bed and are able to travel outside of the mission boundaries.
===Personal relationships===
Missionaries are encouraged to write a letter to their parents weekly. Since almost all of their time is otherwise occupied, other communication is limited. However, a missionary may use preparation day to correspond with any person that is resident outside of the boundaries of the mission. Missionaries do not go on vacation and are generally permitted to telephone their parents only on Christmas Day, and one other day of the year, usually [[Mother's Day]].&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|37}} Missionaries are provided with a free, filtered church e-mail account to correspond with their parents on preparation day only by using a computer in a public location, such as at a [[public library]] or an [[internet café]].&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|20}} In the event of an emergency, family members of a missionary may contact him or her via the mission president's office.
Single missionaries are prohibited from dating or courting while serving missions. The policy of companionships staying together at all times serves to discourage these activities. While missionaries may interact with members of the opposite sex, they may never be alone with them or engage in any kind of intimate physical or emotional activity (e.g., kissing, hugging, holding hands, flirting). They may not telephone, write, e-mail, or accept letters from members of the opposite sex that live in the area where they are assigned to proselyte.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|33}} Missionary companionships are also asked not to visit with members of the opposite sex unless at least one person of the missionaries' same sex is present to chaperone.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|34}} Alternatively, those contacts may be referred to a companionship of the same gender as the contact or to married couple missionaries, when available.
In the early days of the LDS Church, men were called to serve missions regardless of marital status. Today, however, married young men are not expected to serve missions, unless called to oversee a mission as a [[mission president]]. A call to be a mission president is typically extended to the married couple, and in turn, the entire family of the chosen mission president. Older retired couples also may serve as missionaries, but do not take their families with them.

Generally, missionaries wake up at 6:30&amp;nbsp;am.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.mormonnewsroom.org/topic/missionary-program|title=Mormon Missionary Program - Missionaries Serve Two Year Missions|date=July 15, 2014|publisher=|accessdate=February 9, 2017}}&lt;/ref&gt; After praying, exercising (30 minutes), and eating breakfast, they spend two hours studying the scriptures and other materials. If they are teaching in a foreign language, they'll spend another 30 minutes to an hour studying the language. Missionaries leave their place of residence at 10&amp;nbsp;am to proselytize (or 10:30&amp;nbsp;am or 11&amp;nbsp;am if teaching in a foreign language). They have an hour for lunch and dinner, and return to their apartment by 9&amp;nbsp;pm, or 9:30 if they are in the process of teaching a lesson at the end of the day. They plan for the next day's activities, pray, and are encouraged to write in their personal journal, but are not required to. They then retire to bed at 10:30&amp;nbsp;pm.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|14–15}}&lt;ref name=&quot;HandbookOnline&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=https://www.lds.org/manual/missionary-handbook/missionary-conduct?lang=eng|title=Missionary Handbook Missionary Conduct|publisher=|accessdate=February 9, 2017}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Preach My Gospel&quot; /&gt;
On January 25, 2017, the church announced that schedules can be modified depending on the area in which missionaries serve.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web |url=http://www.mormonnewsroom.org/article/more-flexibility-announced-mormon-missionary-schedules |title=More Flexibility Announced for Mormon Missionary Daily Schedules |date=January 25, 2017 |website=Mormon Newsroom |publisher=The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints |language=English |access-date=January 26, 2017}}&lt;/ref&gt;
===Media rules===
Missionaries are admonished to &quot;avoid all forms of worldly entertainment.&quot;&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|24}} Generally, they are not permitted to watch television, listen to the radio, watch or go to movies, or use the Internet&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|25}} (except to use email, and [[social media]] for their work, see [[#Personal relationships|Personal relationships]] above). They are not permitted to listen to music that has romantic lyrics or overtones, or merely entertains.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|25}} The general interpretation of this guideline is to listen to only religious music, such as that performed by the [[Mormon Tabernacle Choir]], though the rule's application has been variable in some missions. They are only permitted to read books, magazines, or other materials authorized by the church.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|27}}
===Slang===
Missionaries are instructed to avoid slang and casual language including when they are alone in their apartment and in their letters to family.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|8–9}} They are also instructed to refer to missionary leaders only by their correct titles.&lt;ref name=&quot;MissionaryHandbook&quot;/&gt;{{rp|9}} However, as with the members of any organization, some missionaries use certain missionary-specific jargon when communicating with one another. Some words and expressions are mission- or language-specific, while others are universal, such as calling the halfway point of a mission the &quot;hump&quot; or [[hump day]],&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book |first= Paul B. |last= Skousen |authorlink= Paul Skousen |title= Brother Paul's Mormon Bathroom Reader |location= Springville, Utah |publisher= Cedar Fort |isbn= 1-55517-895-2 |page= 359}}&lt;/ref&gt; or describing a missionary who is excited about returning home as &quot;trunky&quot; as he has already packed his [[trunk (luggage)|trunk]].{{r|smout1988}}&lt;ref name=&quot;Worthy2008&quot;&gt;{{cite book |last= Worthy |first= Jack B. (pseudonym) |title= The Mormon Cult: A Former Missionary Reveals the Secrets of Mormon Mind Control |year= 2008 |publisher= See Sharp Press (via [[Independent Publishers Group]]) |location= Tucson, AZ |isbn= 1-884365-44-2 |page= 106 |url= http://site.ebrary.com/lib/alltitles/docDetail.action?docID=10326973 }}{{unreliable source?|date=November 2012}}&lt;!-- A self published book, from an author that uses a pseudonym, is perhaps not be best source for this factoid. --&gt;&lt;/ref&gt; Foreign-language missionaries often develop a &quot;mission language&quot;, distinct from but combining aspects of their first and acquired languages, that they use when communicating with each other; the ''[[senkyoshigo]]'' of Japan is an example.{{r|smout1988}}
==Number of missionaries and number of converts==
{{Further|The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints membership history}}
[[File:Ratio of Converts Baptized to Full-Time Missionaries.jpg|thumb|right|490px|Ratio of Converts Baptized to Full-Time Missionaries, 1971–2010]]
As of the end of 2015, there were over 74,000 LDS missionaries serving in 418 [[Mission (LDS Church)|church missions]] throughout the world.&lt;ref name=&quot;Statistics 2015&quot; /&gt; Their work, often in cooperation with local members, resulted in 257,402 convert [[Baptism (Latter Day Saints)|baptisms]] in 2015.&lt;ref name=&quot;Statistics 2015&quot; /&gt; Author David Stewart points out that the number of convert baptisms per missionary per year has fallen from a high of 8.03 in 1989 to just 4.67 in 2005.&lt;ref name= Stewart&gt;{{citation |last= Stewart |first= David G., Jr. |year= 2007 |title= The Law Of The Harvest: Practical Principles of Effective Missionary Work |location= Henderson, NV |publisher= Cumorah Foundation |isbn= 0-9795121-0-7 |oclc= 144460788}}.&lt;/ref&gt; He argues that the number of converts would increase if Mormon missionaries made greater efforts in meeting new people; he points out that the average pair of missionaries spends only four or five hours per week attempting to meet new people.&lt;ref name= Stewart/&gt;
==Types of missionaries==
===Proselyting===
The most visible and most common type of missionaries are typically those who [[proselytism|proselyte]] door-to-door and ride bicycles for transportation. For many years, Mormon missionaries used structured lessons called &quot;missionary discussions&quot; (formally called &quot;The Uniform System for Teaching the Gospel&quot;) to teach interested non-members and recent converts about the doctrines of the LDS Church and to commit them on the steps to take to become a member of the church. Missionaries were often instructed to adhere very closely to the six lessons, and they frequently quoted segments word-for-word (an especially helpful practice when learning a foreign language). The training materials also instructed missionaries to freely change the order of the lessons segments according to the needs and questions of the learners.
The missionary discussions were replaced beginning in October 2004 by a guide called ''Preach My Gospel'' which places emphasis on &quot;teaching by the Spirit&quot;.&lt;ref&gt;{{lds|Doctrine &amp; Covenants|dc|50|14}}&lt;/ref&gt; According to Mormons, &quot;teaching by the Spirit&quot; means seeking guidance from the Holy Ghost to teach; the idea is that the teachings will be catered to each person who is seeking the truth through divine guidance. According to ''Preach My Gospel'', God knows each of His children and can guide His servants to say and teach what is best for each individual.&lt;ref name=&quot;Preach My Gospel&quot;&gt;{{citation |title= Preach My Gospel |year= 2004 |publisher= LDS Church |url= http://www.lds.org/languages/additionalmanuals/preachgospel/PreachMyGospel___00_00_Complete__36617_eng_.pdf}}&lt;/ref&gt;{{rp|89–90}}
Despite the latitude given to missionaries, the guide still contains material which should be actively taught. Chapter 3 of ''Preach My Gospel'' concisely describes all of the doctrine that the missionaries are to teach to those learning about the church. The missionaries are responsible for knowing the doctrine and continually preparing to teach it. They can choose the order that this material is taught to serve the needs of each individual. This is a change from the missionary discussions which were usually taught in order to each investigator.
The book, now published in many languages,&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |url= http://www.lds.org/ensign/2007/11/preach-my-gospel-the-unifying-tool-between-members-and-missionaries?lang=eng |title= Preach My Gospel—the Unifying Tool between Members and Missionaries |authorlink= Erich W. Kopischke |first= Erich W. |last= Kopischke |date=November 2007 |journal= [[Ensign (LDS magazine)|Ensign]] }}&lt;/ref&gt; is meant to be used by the general church membership. This sets it apart from the previous missionary discussions, which were used primarily by full-time missionaries, members with church [[calling (Mormonism)|callings]] related to missionary work, and those preparing to serve missions.{{Citation needed|date=January 2011}}
====Online====
According to ''[[The Huffington Post]]'', the church has online missionaries, who work at a Referral Center Mission in [[Provo, Utah]].&lt;ref name=&quot;huffingtonpost.com&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2014/04/09/mormon-church-online_n_5024251.html|title=Hook Of Mormon: Inside The Church's Online-Only Missionary Army|first1=Bianca Bosker Executive Tech|last1=Editor|first2=The Huffington|last2=Post|date=April 9, 2014|publisher=|accessdate=February 9, 2017}}&lt;/ref&gt; These missionaries use the &quot;Chat with a Mormon&quot; homepage to talk with potential converts.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.mormon.org|title=Mormon.org - What is the Mormon Church and Religion?|publisher=|accessdate=February 9, 2017}}&lt;/ref&gt; The ''[[Salt Lake Tribune]]'' reported that these missionaries are more successful than traditional proselytizers.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.sltrib.com/sltrib/blogsfaithblog/57797283-180/online-missionaries-missionary-mormon.html.csp|title=Online Mormon missionaries outperform traditional proselytizers|first=The Salt Lake|last=Tribune|publisher=|accessdate=February 9, 2017}}&lt;/ref&gt; Furthermore, ''The Huffington Post'' also reported that it is now permissible for missionaries to use [[SMS]], [[blogs]] and [[Facebook]].&lt;ref name=&quot;huffingtonpost.com&quot;/&gt;
===Church-service===
[[File:COUPLE MISSIONNAIRE GENEALOGIE.JPG|thumb|upright|A [[Family History Center (LDS Church)|family history]] missionary couple]]
Missionaries with special needs or health considerations may be called as full-time or part-time service missionaries. Many fully able missionaries are called to do genealogical research or act as tour guides or hosts at [[Temple Square]] or [[Family History Library|Family History libraries]] and other church sites. In many areas, even proselytizing missionaries spend most of their day responding to incoming phone calls and queries, delivering requested media from the church's television and radio commercials. Missionaries may use [[public transit|public transportation]], [[walk]], bicycle, or in some areas drive automobiles owned by the church, or occasionally ride within a private automobile with a church member who is accompanying them to a teaching appointment, proselyting, or [[wikt:fellowshipping|fellowshipping]] activity. At the end of 2015, there were nearly 32,000 church-service missionaries.&lt;ref name=&quot;Statistics 2015&quot; /&gt;
===Humanitarian aid===
The LDS Church also has a strong welfare and humanitarian missionary program. These [[Humanitarian Services|humanitarian missionaries]] typically serve in impoverished areas of the world and do not actively proselytize. Humanitarian missionaries comply with any local laws regarding teaching or displaying religious symbols, including the identifying name tags. This allows them to provide services and aid in countries where activities by religious organizations are typically restricted or forbidden, such as in predominantly Muslim countries or in Southeast Asia. Regular proselytizing missionaries are asked to engage in welfare activities and [[community service]], limited to four hours a week on days other than weekends or preparation day.&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |last= Florence|first= Giles H., Jr. |title= Called to Serve |journal= [[Ensign (LDS magazine)|Ensign]] |page= 13 |date=September 1991 |url= http://www.lds.org/ensign/1991/09/called-to-serve?lang=eng |accessdate= 2012-11-27}}&lt;/ref&gt;
Building missionaries were called by the president of the Tongan Mission in the early 1950s.&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |authorlink= R. Lanier Britsch |first= R. Lanier |last= Britsch |title= The Church in the South Pacific |journal= Ensign |date=February 1976 |page= 19 |url= http://www.lds.org/ensign/1976/02/the-church-in-the-south-pacific?lang=eng |accessdate= 2012-11-27}}&lt;/ref&gt; Among their major successes was building [[Liahona High School]]. From 1955 on, [[Wendell B. Mendenhall]] institutionalized building missionaries on a larger scale with skilled tradesmen called as supervisors of the missionaries. Most of the supervisors were Americans, while most of the workers were young men indigenous to the areas of the South Pacific and Latin America where the work was carried out. However, at times the situation was more complex. One example is Jose Alvarez, who was a native of Argentina, but had lived in the United States for three years when he was called to go with his family to Chile, where he served as a building missionary supervisor.&lt;ref&gt;{{citation |newspaper=[[Church News]] |date= May 12, 2007 |first= John L. |last= Hart |title= 'Always together' — Church, couple matured together: Elder Claudio Zivic always willing to 'go and do' duty |url= http://www.ldschurchnewsarchive.com/articles/50573/Always-together--Church-couple-matured-together.html |accessdate= 2012-11-27}}.&lt;/ref&gt; Often, trainee or assistant building supervisors would work under the leadership of an experienced supervisor in preparation for an assignment as a fully-fledged supervisor of some project or group of missionaries.
==Administration==
===Organization===
{{Main|Mission (LDS Church)}}
Every part of the world is assigned to be within a ''mission'' of the church, whether or not LDS missionaries are active in the area. An adult male [[mission president]] presides over the missionaries in the mission.
Most missions are divided into several ''zones'', a zone being a geographic area specified by the mission president (though these are often the same area as the LDS ecclesiastical unit known as a &quot;stake&quot;). A zone encompasses several more organizational units called ''districts''. Each zone and district is presided over by leaders drawn from male missionaries serving in that area. Zone and district leaders are responsible for gathering weekly statistics, assisting missionaries in their areas of responsibility, and general accountability to the mission president for the well-being and progress of the missionaries under their stewardship. A district typically encompasses four to eight missionaries, and may or may not comprise more than one proselytizing area. An ''area'' is typically a portion of the LDS ecclesiastical unit known as a ward (or congregation), one ward, or multiple wards.
In addition to the leaders mentioned above, the mission president has two or more assistants. Assistants to the President (APs) are typically missionaries who have previously served as district and/or zone leaders. They serve as the president's executive assistants, administering policies and helping missionaries throughout the mission.
===Expenses===
{{see also|Davis v. United States (1990)}}
Missionaries are expected to pay their own expenses while on the mission, often with assistance from family. In the past, each missionary paid his or her actual living expenses, but this approach created a disproportionate burden on missionaries who were assigned to more expensive areas of the world. In 1990, a new program was introduced to equalize the financial responsibility for each missionary and his or her family. Now, all young missionaries pay a flat monthly rate which is then redistributed according to regional costs of living. The cost of a mission varies based on the country of origin of the missionary; for missionaries from the United States, the cost is US$400 per month.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|last1=Bolingbroke|first1=Bruce|title=Benefits of equal costs for LDS missions|url=http://www.deseretnews.com/article/865625380/Benefits-of-equal-costs-for-LDS-missions.html?pg=all|accessdate=30 March 2016|publisher=Deseret News|date=2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[[Peggy Fletcher Stack]], [http://www.sltrib.com/sltrib/blogsfaithblog/56404563-180/church-lds-missionaries-missionary.html.csp &quot;Rising Mormon missionary numbers spur call for more donations&quot;], ''[[The Salt Lake Tribune]]'', 3 June 2013. The cost can vary dramatically based on the country of origin. For instance, in Oceania, missionaries from Australia pay AU$395 and missionaries from New Zealand pay NZ$400, while missionaries from American Samoa pay US$85 and missionaries from Kiribati pay AU$20. Missionaries from Papua New Guinea, Solomon Islands, and Tuvalu are exempt from making any payment: Pacific Area Presidency, &quot;Financing Full-time Missionary Service&quot; (letter to Area Seventies, Stake, Mission, and District Presidents; Bishops and Branch Presidents), 28 August 2014.&lt;/ref&gt; The monthly rate helps to cover food, lodging, transportation, and other mission related expenses. Missionaries are asked to bring extra personal money for any personal items they would like to purchase. Once the money is received by the church it is then redistributed to the missionaries in amounts proportionate to the cost of living within the assigned mission area. {{Citation needed|date=July 2007}} As families now contribute to a general fund for missionary expenses, the sum is deductible under many nations' tax policies regarding charitable gifts.
For health care, the church provides missionaries with limited medical care. A missionary will be required to pay for any medical treatment that is considered non-essential or that is considered to be associated with a preexisting condition. The local mission office will often help missionaries find Mormon doctors or dentists who can offer their services to missionaries for a small fee or for free.
Young people in the church are encouraged to save money throughout their childhood and teenage years to pay for as much of their mission as they can, although many receive assistance from parents, family, or friends. Missionaries who cannot save the required funds may obtain assistance from their home [[Ward (LDS Church)|congregation]] or from a general missionary fund operated by the church and contributed to by Latter-day Saints around the world. Married couple missionaries are expected to pay their own costs,&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.lds.org/csm/faq.pdf |title=FAQ: Mission and Service Opportunities for Senior Adults and Recommended Young Adults |publisher=LDS Church |format=PDF |accessdate=November 12, 2008 |deadurl=yes |archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20081029040248/http://www.lds.org/csm/faq.pdf |archivedate=October 29, 2008 }}&lt;/ref&gt; but in 2011 the church began paying for missionary couples' housing expenses that exceed US$1400 per month.&lt;ref name= chasehall/&gt; In many areas, church members often invite locally assigned missionaries over for meals to help reduce the overall expenditures of the missionary program.

A returned missionary (often abbreviated &quot;RM&quot;) is a term used by members of the LDS Church to refer to men and women who have previously served as Mormon missionaries. Once they return home, RMs are generally encouraged to begin dating seriously and to seek marriage.&lt;ref&gt;[[Spencer W. Kimball]], [http://www.lds.org/ensign/1975/02/the-marriage-decision “The Marriage Decision,”] ''[[Ensign (LDS magazine)|Ensign]]'', February 1975, p. 2.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[[Ezra Taft Benson]], [http://www.lds.org/ensign/1988/05/to-the-single-adult-brethren-of-the-church “To the Single Adult Brethren of the Church,”] ''[[Ensign (LDS magazine)|Ensign]]'', May 1988, p. 51.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[[Earl C. Tingey]], [http://www.lds.org/liahona/2007/04/three-messages-to-young-adults “Three Messages to Young Adults,”] ''[[Liahona (magazine)|Liahona]]'', April 2007, pp. 26–31.&lt;/ref&gt; Those who learned to speak a foreign language must readjust, sometimes with difficulty, to speaking their [[first language]].&lt;ref name=&quot;smout1988&quot;&gt;{{cite journal | title=Senkyoshigo: A Missionary English of Japan | author=Smout, Kary D. | journal=American Speech |date=Summer 1988 | volume=63 | issue=2 | pages=137–149 | jstor=454418 | doi=10.2307/454418}}&lt;/ref&gt;
In Mormon culture, stereotypes and jokes abound regarding newly returned missionaries, most dealing with their difficulties in handling the reverse culture shock. Other stereotypes revolve around the fact that as missionaries, they lived highly structured, disciplined lives and avoided contact with members of the opposite sex, so many RMs have difficulty readjusting to social life and dating.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.lightplanet.com/mormons/daily/missionary/index.htm |title=Mormon Missionary Work |publisher=Lightplanet.com |accessdate=June 7, 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Other stereotypes include the supposed rush of many RMs to get married as soon as possible. Many families whose daughters are old enough to marry encourage them to date RMs.
Returned missionaries are frequently called to assist in the local missionary effort and are encouraged to stay active within the LDS Church through callings and service.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://lds.about.com/od/mormonmissionaries |title=Mormon Missionaries |publisher=Lds.about.com |date=May 20, 2011 |accessdate=June 7, 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; RMs who served in the same mission frequently stay in touch and often gather for mission reunions in Utah to coincide with the semiannual [[LDS General Conference]].&lt;ref name=&quot;desnewsreunions&quot;&gt;{{cite web | url=http://www.deseretnews.com/reunions/s-list/1,2505,A,00.html | title=Current LDS mission reunion information | publisher=Deseret News | accessdate=October 2, 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;
The notion of the Mormon mission as a crucible is a common one, and the benefits gained from going through it have been used to help explain the prominence of LDS Church members in business and civic life.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news | url=http://magazine.byu.edu/?act=view&amp;a=847 | title=The Saint-Making Experience | author=Cracroft, Richard A. | magazine=[[BYU Magazine]] | date=Winter 2002}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web | url=http://www.pbs.org/mormons/etc/synopsis.html | title=Introduction | work=[[The Mormons (documentary)|The Mormons]] | publisher=[[Public Broadcasting Service|PBS]] | date=April 30, 2007}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news | url=http://www.businessweek.com/magazine/content/11_25/b4233058977933.htm | title=God's MBAs: Why Mormon Missions Produce Leaders | author=Winter, Caroline | magazine=[[Bloomberg Businessweek]] | date=June 9, 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news| url=https://www.nytimes.com/2007/11/15/us/politics/15romney.html | title=Romney, Searching and Earnest, Set His Path in ’60s | author=Kirkpatrick, David D. | newspaper=[[The New York Times]] | date=November 15, 2007 | accessdate=November 16, 2007 | page=A1}}&lt;/ref&gt; Mission experience  has also helped prepare RMs for later engaging and prospering in non-Mormon environments.&lt;ref name = &quot;NewsMorm&quot;&gt;{{cite news | author=[[Walter Kirn|Kirn, Walter]] | date=June 5, 2011 | url=http://www.newsweek.com/2011/06/05/mormons-rock.print.html | title=Mormons Rock! | magazine=[[Newsweek]] | accessdate=July 21, 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;

==History==
[[File:Mormons visit a country carpenter.jpg|thumb|left|&quot;Mormons visit a country carpenter&quot; (1856) by [[Christen Dalsgaard]], depicting a mid-19th century visit of a missionary to a Danish carpenter's workshop. The first missionaries arrived in Denmark in 1850.]]
{{Main|Mission (LDS Church)#History of missions}}
{{See also|List of LDS missionary entries by country}}
{{Expand section|date=May 2011}}
The LDS Church regards [[Samuel H. Smith (Latter Day Saints)|Samuel H. Smith]], the younger brother of church founder [[Joseph Smith]], as the church's first full-time missionary.&lt;ref&gt;Ryan Carr, [http://lds.org/liahona/2004/09/the-first-latter-day-missionary &quot;The First Latter-day Missionary&quot;], ''[[Liahona (magazine)|Liahona]]'', Sep. 2004.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Karianne Salisbury, [http://www.lds.org/liahona/2008/09/news-of-the-church/first-latter-day-saint-missionary-samuel-smith-born-200-years-ago &quot;News of the Church: First Latter-day Saint Missionary, Samuel Smith, Born 200 Years Ago&quot;], ''[[Liahona (magazine)|Liahona]]'', Sep. 2008.&lt;/ref&gt; Smith traveled from city to city, covering more than 4,000 miles, trying to sell copies of the [[Book of Mormon]]. The book was presented as evidence of Joseph Smith's prophetic calling. [[Phineas Young]] received a Book of Mormon from Samuel Smith and became an early member of the church. (Phineas' younger brother [[Brigham Young|Brigham]] would later be baptized and become the 2nd president of the LDS Church.)
During the 1850s missionaries were sent to Chile, France, Germany, Hawaii, India, Italy, Scandinavia, Switzerland and a number of other countries.&lt;ref name=&quot;mormonnewsroom.org&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=History of Missionary Work in the Church|url=http://www.mormonnewsroom.org/article/history-of-missionary-work-in-the-church|accessdate=23 November 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;
In 1898, the church began allowing single women to be called as missionaries. The first two single female missionaries were [[Jennie Brimhall]] and [[Inez Knight]], who were called to serve as companions in England.&lt;ref name = mangum&gt;Diane L. Mangum, [http://www.lds.org/ensign/1980/07/the-first-sister-missionaries &quot;The First Sister Missionaries&quot;], ''[[Ensign (LDS magazine)|Ensign]]'', July 1980.&lt;/ref&gt;
Each mission was responsible for individually developing lessons until 1952 when, under the direction of [[Gordon B. Hinkley]], the church published &quot;A Systematic Program for Teaching the Gospel&quot;.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|last1=Duffy|first1=John-Charles|title=The New Missionary Discussions and the Future of Correlation|journal=Sunstone|url=https://www.sunstonemagazine.com/pdf/138-28-46.pdf|accessdate=23 November 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 1961, towards the beginning of the church's [[Priesthood Correlation Program|correlation]] effort, &quot;A Uniform System for Teaching Investigators&quot; was made mandatory church-wide. The missionary lessons, or discussions as they were often called, continued to be updated with major revisions approximately every decade up until the publication of ''Preach My Gospel'' in 2004. Early missionaries, like Samuel Smith, relied primarily on the story of the Book of Mormon to attract converts to the church. By the late 19th century, the story of Joseph Smith's first vision had gained prominence and was given an important role in the official missionary lessons.&lt;ref&gt;&quot;Historians have pondered the various phrases of this vision's evolution and tend to see its present form as a 'late development,' only gaining an influential status in LDS self-reflection late in the nineteenth century.&quot; {{citation |first=Douglas J. |last=Davies |title=An Introduction to Mormonism |publisher=Cambridge University Press |year=2003 |page=136}}; {{harvnb|Widmer|2000|pp=92–107}}; {{harvnb|Shipps|1985|pp=30–32}}.&lt;/ref&gt;
In the early 1950s, [[Henry D. Moyle]] had responsibility over the missionary department. Moyle encouraged missionaries to use a tactic that later became known as &quot;baseball baptism&quot;. Missionaries 
would encourage young men to join church-sponsored sports leagues and baptism was imposed as a prerequisite.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|last1=Quinn|first1=D. Michael|title=The Mormon 'Baseball Baptism' Era|url=http://mormonstories.org/baseballbaptisms.html|accessdate=22 November 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; The use of this tactic created a large number of converts, especially in England, but very few of the young men ever had any interest in the spiritual aspects of the church. Moyle introduced baptism goals, missionary incentives and other salesmanship techniques into the church missionary department. [[David O. McKay]] eventually removed Moyle from his responsibilities over the missionary program but many of the changes introduced during this time remain even today.
During the 1960s, missionaries whose area of service required them to learn a new language were first sent to the Language Training Mission on the [[BYU]] campus. This later became the [[Missionary Training Center]] in 1978 where all new missionaries were sent for training not just the ones learning a language.
Starting during [[Spencer W. Kimball]]'s administration, it was emphasized that &quot;every able, worthy young man&quot; had a duty to serve a mission.&lt;ref name=&quot;mormonnewsroom.org&quot;/&gt; Prior to this, missionary service for men was not viewed as obligatory. This resulted in an increase in the number of young men choosing to serve missions.
Before [[1978 Revelation on Priesthood|1978]], the church did not permit people of African descent to hold the priesthood, and they were not actively proselytized. Missionary work in places such as [[Brazil]] may have increased pressure to change the policy due to the difficulty of verifying lack of African descent in these populations.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|last1=Kimball|first1=Edward|title=Spencer W. Kimball and the Revelation on Priesthood|accessdate=20 Feb 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; When the policy was eliminated by Kimball, additional areas of the world were opened up to missionary work.
Church researchers discovered that in addition to the content of the lessons, the way in which the material was presented had a major impact on conversion. In 1984, the Missionary Department codified the &quot;commitment pattern&quot; as the proper way for missionaries to teach the lessons.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|last1=Shepherd|first1=Gordon|last2=Shepherd|first2=Gary|title=Membership Growth, Church Activity, and Missionary Recruitment|journal=Dialogue|url=https://dialoguejournal.com/wp-content/uploads/sbi/articles/Dialogue_V29N01_39.pdf|accessdate=23 November 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; This involves the missionaries making specific invitations to act in response to the lessons. Gospel students would be encouraged to make personal commitments to attend church, to read the Book of Mormon and to be baptized.
In 2002, apostle [[M. Russell Ballard]] delivered a [[General Conference (LDS Church)|General Conference]] address stating that the bar to qualify for missionary service had been raised and that &quot;the day of the 'repent and go' missionary is over&quot;.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ballard&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.lds.org/general-conference/2002/10/the-greatest-generation-of-missionaries|title=The Greatest Generation of Missionaries|last=Ballard|first=M. Russell|date=October 2002|publisher=LDS Church|accessdate=March 5, 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;
During the church's October 2012 General Conference, church president [[Thomas S. Monson]] announced that the minimum age for missionary service for young men had been lowered from 19 to 18 and that the minimum age for young women had been lowered from 21 to 19.&lt;ref name = missionaryAge/&gt; Immediately following the announcement, the church experienced an unprecedented influx of new missionaries. The rate of new missionaries swelled &quot;by 471 percent, from about 700 new applications per week to about 4,000 each week, with young women comprising more than half of the new applicants.&quot; Later, the number of missionaries applying to serve slowed, but it is still double what it had been in the past and now the applicants are split somewhat evenly among young women and young men.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web |url=http://www.deseretnews.com/article/865570142/ |title=Numbers of new LDS missionaries 'unprecedented,' church says |last1=Walker |first1=Joseph |last2= |first2= |date=January 7, 2013 |website= |publisher=''[[Deseret News]]'' |accessdate=April 23, 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 2013, the number of missionaries peaked at 89,000&lt;ref name=&quot;New Missions for 2015&quot;&gt;{{citation |first= R. Scott |last= Lloyd |date= January 9, 2015 |title= LDS Church announces 11 new missions, 2015 mission president assignments |url= http://www.deseretnews.com/article/865619186/Creation-of-11-new-missions-indicates-the-work-continues-apace.html?pg=all |newspaper= [[Church News]] }}&lt;/ref&gt; and had dropped to 74,000 by the end of 2015.&lt;ref name=&quot;Statistics 2015&quot; /&gt; Throughout the [[History of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints|history of the church]], over one million missionaries have been sent on missions.&lt;ref name = onemillion1/&gt;&lt;ref name = onemillion2/&gt;
===Incidents===
[[File:JBBall Gravestone.jpg|thumb|Headstone of a missionary that was murdered by the [[Zarate Willka Armed Forces of Liberation]] while serving in Bolivia.{{r|jacobsenwells19890525}}]]
Although rare, missionaries have been the victims of violence. In 1974, two young-adult male missionaries were murdered in [[Austin, Texas]] by [[Robert Elmer Kleason]]. In 1977, the [[Mormon sex in chains case|case]] of a Mormon missionary who said he was abducted and raped by a woman was covered extensively by newspapers in Britain, being dubbed the [[Mormon sex in chains case]].&lt;ref&gt;Delano, Anthony (1978). ''Joyce McKinney and the Manacled Mormon''. Mirror Books. ISBN 978-0-85939-140-5.&lt;/ref&gt; In 1989, the [[Zarate Willka Armed Forces of Liberation]] killed two American missionaries in Bolivia.&lt;ref name=jacobsenwells19890525&gt;{{cite news | url=http://www.deseretnews.com/article/print/48103/2-LDS-MISSIONARIES-ASSASSINATED-IN-BOLIVIA-TERRORISTS-CLAIM-RESPONSIBILITY-FOR-POLITICALLY-MOTIVATED.html | title=2 LDS Missionaries Assassinated in Bolivia / Terrorists Claim Responsibility for Politically Motivated Killing | work=The Deseret News | date=May 25, 1989 | accessdate=November 28, 2011 |author1=Jacobsen-Wells, JoAnn |author2=Spangler, Jerry }}&lt;/ref&gt; From 1999 to 2006, three LDS missionaries were murdered worldwide, while 22 died in accidents of some sort.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.foxnews.com/story/0,2933,180413,00.html Mormon Missionary Shot Dead in Virginia] from ''[[Fox News]]''&lt;/ref&gt; In 2008, three men from [[Port Shepstone]], South Africa were convicted of [[rape|raping]] and [[robbery|robbing]] two female LDS missionaries in June 2006.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.news24.com/World/News/Missionary-rapists-jailed-20080125 &quot;Missionaries' rapists convicted&quot;], ''[[Pretoria News]]'', 2008-01-24.&lt;/ref&gt;
A few cases of kidnapping have also occurred, a recent one being in 1998, when two male missionaries were abducted while working in the [[Samara Oblast|Samara]] region of Russia. The kidnappers demanded US$300,000 for their return. The missionaries were released unharmed a few days later without payment of the ransom.&lt;ref&gt;[http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/europe/68320.stm Kidnapped Mormons freed] from the ''[[BBC]]''&lt;/ref&gt; 
In August 2006, three male missionaries from Idaho, Nevada and California participated in the vandalism of a Roman Catholic shrine in [[San Luis, Colorado]], for which desecration the LDS Church apologized shortly thereafter.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.wnd.com/2008/03/59802/ Vandalism case against Mormons dropped: Catholic bishop urges Christian forgiveness for desecration of shrine] from [[WorldNetDaily]]&lt;/ref&gt; The incident recalled a 1972 occurrence in which a pair of missionaries in Thailand took pictures of themselves sitting on an ancient [[Gautama Buddha|Buddha]] statue. Although the missionaries &quot;probably didn't think much about it&quot;,&lt;ref name = byugaffe/&gt; they were caught and sentenced to a year in prison, and their images were published in the newspapers. The [[Bhumibol Adulyadej|King of Thailand]] pardoned them on his birthday, and they were released after six months. Missionaries of the church are counseled to respect other religions and cultures, one reason being to avoid such conflicts.&lt;ref name = byugaffe&gt;[http://newsnet.byu.edu/story.cfm/68043 Proselytizing History Repeats with Recent Missionary Gaffe] from ''BYU NewsNet''&lt;/ref&gt;
==In popular culture==
Mormon missionaries have been portrayed in various popular culture media. Missionaries are the main focus of [[LDS cinema]] films ''[[God's Army (motion picture)|God's Army]]'' (1999), ''[[The Other Side of Heaven]]'' (2001), ''[[The Best Two Years]]'' (2003), ''[[The R.M.]]'' (2003), ''[[God's Army 2|God's Army 2: States of Grace]]'' (2005), ''[[Return with Honor (2007)|Return with Honor]]'' (2007), ''[[The Errand of Angels]]'' (2008) and ''[[The Saratov Approach]]'' (2013). The musical ''[[Saturday's Warrior]]'' (1973) features missionaries and was made into a film in 1989. The DVD series ''[[Liken the Scriptures]]'' occasionally shows missionaries.
Missionaries were featured in the [[Public Broadcasting Service|PBS]] documentary ''[[Get the Fire]]'' (2003), as well as in the [[Tony Award]]-winning satirical Broadway musical ''[[The Book of Mormon (musical)|The Book of Mormon]]''. Hollywood portrayed missionaries in ''[[Yes Man (film)|Yes Man]]'' (2008) starring [[Jim Carrey]], and British film ''[[Millions (2004 film)|Millions]]'' also mentioned missionaries.
Films portraying missionaries gone astray include ''[[Trapped by the Mormons]]'' (1922), ''[[Orgazmo]]'' (1997) and ''[[Latter Days]]'' (2003). Mormon missionaries appeared at the end of the American [[horror film]] ''[[The Strangers (2008 film)|The Strangers]]'' (2008); the missionaries were depicted as children as opposed to young men.
In 2008, former missionary Chad Hardy was subjected to [[Disciplinary council|church discipline]] after releasing a [[pin-up]] calendar titled &quot;Men on a Mission&quot;, which consisted of pictures of scantily clad returned missionaries.&lt;ref&gt;Lilly Fowler, [http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/content/article/2007/10/05/AR2007100502146.html &quot;Calendar Shows Another Side of Mormons&quot;], ''[[Washington Post]]'', 2007-10-06, p. B09.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Ali Velazquez, [http://newsnet.byu.edu/story.cfm/65445 &quot;12 Former LDS Missionaries Posing for Controversial Calendar&quot;], ''BYU NewsNet'', 2007-09-24.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name = beefcake&gt;Steve Friess, [http://www.newsweek.com/id/147061/page/1 &quot;Mormon Beefcake: Did a frisky calendar get an LDS member excommunicated?&quot;], ''[[Newsweek]]'', 2008-07-18.&lt;/ref&gt;

      <text xml:space="preserve">[[File:Eddie Dowling 1950.jpg|thumb|Dowling in 1950]]
'''Eddie Dowling''' (December 11, 1889&lt;ref name=&quot;baptismal&quot;&gt;Date and year of birth as per baptismal records of Precious Blood church, [[Woonsocket, Rhode Island]], where Dowling was christened&lt;/ref&gt; — February 18, 1976) was an [[United States|American]] [[actor]], [[screenwriter]], [[playwright]], director, [[theatrical producer|producer]], [[songwriter]] and [[composer]].
==Biography==
Born '''Joseph Goucher''' on December 11, 1889, he was the 14th of 17 children born to a father of French-Canadian descent and a mother of Irish descent in [[Woonsocket, Rhode Island]]. His middle name was either &quot;Narcisse&quot; or &quot;Nelson&quot; (varying sources). He took his professional surname from the maiden name of his mother, Bridget Mary Dowling, who was born in [[Smithfield, Rhode Island]]. His father was Charles Goucher who was born in [[Québec, Canada|St Marcel, Province of Québec, Canada]].&lt;ref name=&quot;baptismal&quot; /&gt;
He appeared on stage for many years, including appearances in the [[Ziegfeld Follies]].&lt;ref name=cullen&gt;Cullen, Frank and Hackman, Florence (2006). ''Vaudeville, Old and New: An Encyclopedia of Variety Performers in America'', pp. 322-23. Routledge. ISBN 0-415-93853-8.&lt;/ref&gt; His most famous role was as &quot;Tom Wingfield&quot; in the original [[Broadway theatre|Broadway]] production of ''[[The Glass Menagerie]]'', starring opposite [[Laurette Taylor]] and [[Julie Haydon]]. He produced the play's original Chicago production in 1944, and followed it to Broadway.&lt;ref name=cullen/&gt;
Dowling sought the 1934 [[Democratic Party (United States)|Democratic]] nomination for the [[United States Senate]] seat from [[Rhode Island]]. At the time, [[TIME magazine]] reported that his great-grandfather and two great-grand uncles were the founders of [[Goucher College]] for Women in Baltimore, and that &quot;he was the 14th in a family of 17 children; his schooling had extended only up to the third grade; he had been a cabin boy and a music hall singer ... and he owned a sausage factory in California.&quot;''&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.time.com/time/magazine/article/0,9171,754076,00.html ''Time'' magazine description of Dowling]&lt;/ref&gt;
He was married to the [[Glasgow, Scotland]]-born actress and stage performer, [[Rachel Rice Dooley]] (October 30, 1888 — January 28, 1984) who specialized in [[physical comedy]]; they had two children, Jack and Maxine. Jack Dowling died serving in the World War II.
Eddie Dowling died in [[Smithfield, Rhode Island]] in 1976, at the age of 86. His widow died in 1984, aged 95, in [[East Hampton (village), New York|East Hampton, Long Island, New York]].

'''KFRH''' (104.3 [[FM broadcasting|FM]]) – branded as '''104.3 Now FM''' – is a commercial [[radio station]] in [[North Las Vegas, Nevada]], broadcasting to the [[Las Vegas, Nevada]] area on 104.3 [[FM Broadcasting|FM]].
KFRH airs a [[Contemporary Hit Radio|Top 40 (CHR)]] music format branded as &quot;104.3 Now FM&quot; with the slogan &quot;Vegas' New Hit Music Channel&quot;.
Its studios are in the unincorporated [[Clark County, Nevada|Clark County]] community of [[Enterprise, Nevada|Enterprise]] and the transmitter is on Potosi Mountains west of the Las Vegas Valley.
==History==
===Before 102.7 (1999–2009)===
KFRH was originally located at the 105.5 frequency and featured a Hot AC Format, in [[1999 in radio|1999]], a frequency swap took place in which a new frequency of 105.7 moved to the Las Vegas area and KFRH (which was KSTJ at the time) moved to the 102.7 frequency as a result. It had also debuted a 1980s hits format which carried moderate success, but in 2005, KMZQ-FM (now [[KXQQ-FM]]) debuted the Jack FM concept, with similar music being played on 100.5, with a bigger playlist, plus the shrinking of the 1980s formats in general Beasley decided to move KFRH back to a Hot AC format. On September 14, 2007 at 3PM, the station rebranded as &quot;Fresh 102.7&quot;. The last song on Star was &quot;[[Cum On Feel the Noize]]&quot; by [[Quiet Riot]] (a nod to the previous 80s hits format), whilst the first song on Fresh was &quot;[[Straight from the Heart (song)|Straight From The Heart]]&quot; by [[Bryan Adams]].&lt;ref&gt;[https://radioinsight.com/headlines/63/fresh-1027-las-vegas/]&lt;/ref&gt; The station's callsign was changed from '''KSTJ''' to KFRH to reflect the new format on October 5, [[2007 in radio|2007]].  Even though KFRH stayed [[hot adult contemporary]]; [[Mediabase]] had it on the hot AC panel before shifting to [[contemporary hit radio]].
===102.7 Now (2009)===
On April 1, [[2009 in radio|2009]] at 2PM, KFRH dropped its Hot AC format and began [[stunting (broadcasting)|stunting]] with a loop of voices asking &quot;When?&quot; followed by the occasional &quot;Soon&quot;, along with several songs with the word &quot;when&quot; in the title. At 4:33 PM, the station flipped to a [[Top 40]] format branded as '''102.7 Now''' with a commitment to play 102 straight minutes of uninterrupted music. The first song played on 102.7 Now was &quot;[[Right Now (Na Na Na)]]&quot; by [[Akon]].&lt;ref&gt;[https://radioinsight.com/headlines/netgnomes/856/fresh-102-7-kfrh-las-vegas-flipping-when/ “Fresh 102.7” KFRH Las Vegas Becomes CHR “102.7 Now”]&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[https://formatchange.com/fresh-1027-las-vegas-becomes-1027-now/ “Fresh 102.7” Las Vegas Becomes “102.7 Now”]&lt;/ref&gt; There are also two other commercial radio stations in the United States that are branded similar, those being [[WBMP (FM)|WNOW]] &quot;92.3 Now FM&quot; in [[New York City|New York]], and [[KAMP-FM]] &quot;97.1 AMP Radio&quot; in [[Los Angeles]]. The station launched by playing music for 24 hours without commercial interruption. This was following the original campaign of playing &quot;10,000 Songs in a Row&quot;. The station also claimed to offer less commercials every hour than [[rhythmic contemporary]] competitor &quot;[[KLUC-FM|98.5 KLUC]]&quot;.
===104.3 Now FM (2009–present)===
The station switched to 104.3 from 102.7 on August 25, 2009. The station switched with sister Country station '''104.3 The Coyote''' ([[KCYE]]), which moved to the previous 102.7 frequency. The station leaned to a more Rhythmic format when it was previously broadcasting on 102.7, but has now changed to a complete Top 40 format adding more artists like The Weeknd, Calvin Harris, Ariana Grande, Sia, Coldplay, Justin Timberlake, Rihanna, Twenty-One Pilots, Bruno Mars, Ed Sheeran, Adele and Taylor Swift to compete with cross-town rival 98.5 KLUC, which also in 2008 leaned to a more CHR Top 40 format from previously broadcasting a Rhythmic format. Future plans also call for a call sign change, and on-air personalities will be added to the station.
==HD radio ==
KFRH airs the [[adult contemporary]] format formerly featured on the main channel on its [[HD Radio|HD2]] signal branded as &quot; Fresh FM.&quot; The third HD channel rebroadcasts [[NOAA Weather Radio]]'s [[WXL36]] to provide that service in an FM dial form.

'''Jim Krueger''' is an [[People of the United States|American]] [[comic book writer]], [[novelist]], and [[filmmaker]].
==Early life==
Krueger graduated from [[Marquette University]] with a degree in [[journalism]].
==Career==
=== Filmmaking ===
Krueger's first short film, ''[[They Might Be Dragons]]'', which he wrote, directed, and produced, won &quot;Best In Class&quot; at [[New York University]] (NYU), a &quot;Best Short Film&quot; award from the [[New York International Independent Film and Video Festival|New York Independent Film Festival]], &lt;ref&gt;&quot;The Creators,&quot; Avengers/Invaders Sketchbook (Marvel Comics, 2008).&lt;/ref&gt; and a SILVER from the [[Crested Butte Film Festival]].  He directed the Radio City Rockettes in the musical ''Eleven''.  Krueger has also written the story script for Midway Games' ''[[Mortal Kombat Shaolin Monks]]'' video game, and won the Satellite Award for Best Action/Adventure Video Game. 
=== Comics ===
Krueger won the Eisner award for Best Graphic Novel and was a New York Times Bestseller for writing &quot;[[Justice (DC Comics)]]&quot; for DC Comics.  He was named one of the top ten writers in comics and an innovator in the field by Wizard Magazine. 
Krueger won two [[Addy Awards]] during his first year as a [[copywriter]]. A year later he became a [[creative director]] at [[Marvel Comics]], and has since become a [[freelance]] comic book writer/property creator.
His original works include ''[[The Foot Soldiers]]'', ''Alphabet Supes'', ''[[The Clock Maker]]'', ''The High Cost of Happily Ever After'' and ''The Last Straw Man''.
Other comic book writing includes the award-winning ''[[Earth X]]'' trilogy with [[Alex Ross]] for [[Marvel Comics]], as well as ''[[Avengers (comics)|Avengers]]'', ''[[X-Men]]'', ''[[Star Wars]]'', ''[[The Matrix Comics]]'', ''[[Micronauts (comics)|Micronauts]]'', ''[[Galactic]]'', and ''[[Batman]]''.  Krueger And Alex Ross co wrote [[Justice (DC Comics)|Justice]]'', and ''Avengers/Invaders'', [[Project Superpowers]] (dynamite press)
He privately wrote  ''Capone vs. Dracula''. and he has since introduced two new comic book formats.
His book The Frankincense Monster and Other Haunted Christmas Stories was read for charity for the Los Angeles Children's Hospital.
Krueger is also president and publisher of his own comic book/entertainment company, 26 Soldiers.

      <text xml:space="preserve">{{Refimprove|date=April 2014}}
'''IntegRate''' was a [[software]] product written in [[C++]] based on a pipe-lined high performance [[architecture (disambiguation)|architecture]] for handling batch [[rating of telecommunications]] using [[call detail record]]s (CDR), developed by the German software company Solution 42.
==Description==
The product was an early [[massively parallel (computing)|massively parallel]] application. It processed and charge thousands of events per second on commodity hardware. The software was able to run on the following operating systems: [[Windows]], [[Unix]]/[[Linux]], [[Solaris (operating system)|Solaris]], [[HP-UX]] and [[AIX]]. It contained algorithms for ultra-fast [[regular expression]] matching using a [[finite-state machine]] as well as one of the first [[in-memory database]]s.
IntegRate platform is a conceptual structure for performing the complex task of [[telecommunications rating]] and price modeling. The &quot;framework&quot; contains &quot;pipelines&quot;, each of which consists of &quot;[[Plug-in (computing)|plug-in]]s&quot;, which can be freely combined. The standard plug-ins cover rating tasks (e.g. zoning, service determination) and the overall rating process can be customized by using the IntegRate script languages &quot;iScript&quot; and &quot;iRule&quot;. This allows configuring plug-in modules to prepare or post-process CDR-information.
Solution42 (a name taken from [[Douglas Adams]]'s &quot;[[Hitchhikers Guide to the Galaxy]]&quot; cult series), originally based in [[Quickborn]], [[Germany]], was purchased by [[Portal Software]] in 2000.&lt;ref&gt;https://www.nytimes.com/2000/11/04/business/company-news-portal-software-in-deal-to-acquire-solution-42.html&lt;/ref&gt; Since the purchase of Solution42, the IntegRate architecture was integrated into the so-called &quot;Portal Infranet&lt;ref&gt;http://www.softscout.com/software/Accounting-and-Finance/Bill-and-Invoice-Generation/Infranet.html&lt;/ref&gt;&quot; telecommications billing platform, now called the Batch Rating Engine (BRE).
In 2006 [[Oracle Corporation]] acquired Portal Software.&lt;ref&gt;http://www.oracle.com/us/corporate/Acquisitions/portalsoftware/index.html&lt;/ref&gt; IntegRate became the central rating and mediation engine with Oracle's CBRM System.&lt;ref&gt;http://www.oracle.com/us/products/applications/communications/billing-revenue-management/overview/index.html&lt;/ref&gt;
In 2003 the core engineers and lead architects of former Solution42 founded ENTEREST&lt;ref&gt;http://www.enterest.eu/&lt;/ref&gt; to build the successor product &quot;EDR Workbench&quot; marketed for [[complex event processing]] using the marketing term [[big data]].
