      <format>text/x-wiki</format>
      <text xml:space="preserve">'''Pauline Flanagan''' (29 June 1925 – 28 June 2003) was a [[County Sligo]]-born [[Irish people|Irish]] [[actor|actress]] who had a long career on [[theatre|stage]]. [[United States|American]] television audiences best knew her as Maeve Ryan's sister, Annie Colleary, on the [[soap opera]] ''[[Ryan's Hope]]'' in 1979 and again in 1981. She later returned to the show as Sister Mary Joel.
==Life==
Her family was deeply political and supported the Republican (anti-Treaty) side during the [[Irish Civil War]]. Both of her parents, Patrick and Elizabeth (née Mulligan) Flanagan, both served as Lord Mayor of Sligo.&lt;ref name=play&gt;Simonson, Robert. [http://www.playbill.com/news/article/pauline-flanagan-irish-character-actress-is-dead-114053# &quot;Pauline Flanagan, Irish Character Actress, Is Dead&quot;] Playbill, 30 June 2003.&lt;/ref&gt; She was good friends with fellow Irish actresses [[Joan O'Hara]] and [[Paddy Croft]]. Flanagan spent much of the early 1950s touring with [[Anew McMaster]], where she met [[Harold Pinter]] at the Gate's Pinter Festival.&lt;ref&gt;McGarry, Patsy. &quot;Theatre world mourns Pauline Flanagan&quot;, ''The Irish Times'', July 1, 2003&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://clancybrothersandtommymakem.com/trad_0501_countess.htm|title=The Countess Cathleen|publisher=Topic|work=Sleeve notes|accessdate=20 April 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;
==Career==
She appeared in many Broadway plays, starting in 1957 with Dylan Thomas' ''[[Under Milk Wood]]''.&lt;ref name=play/&gt; She starred in the 1976 Broadway revival of ''[[The Innocents (play)|The Innocents]]''.&lt;ref name=vault&gt;[http://www.playbillvault.com/Person/Detail/67907/Pauline-Flanagan &quot;Pauline Flanagan Broadway Listing&quot;] playbillvault.com, accessed September 13, 2015&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.playbillvault.com/Show/Detail/2391/The-Innocents &quot;'The Innocents' Broadway 1976&quot;] playbillvault.com, accessed September 13, 2015&lt;/ref&gt; She appeared on Broadway in ''[[Philadelphia, Here I Come!]]'' in 1994.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.ibdb.com/production.php?id=4268 ''Philadelphia, Here I Come!'' 1994] ibdb, accessed September 13, 2015&lt;/ref&gt;
She appeared [[Off-Broadway]], several times with the [[Irish Repertory Theatre]], including ''[[Juno and the Paycock]]'' (1995)&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.lortel.org/lla_archive/index.cfm?search_by=show&amp;id=732 ''Juno and the Paycock'' 1995] lortel.org, accessed September 13, 2015&lt;/ref&gt; and ''A Life'' (2001)&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.lortel.org/lla_archive/index.cfm?search_by=show&amp;id=80 ''A Life'' 2001] lortel.org, accessed September 13, 2015&lt;/ref&gt; She appeared in the [[Harold Prince]] play ''Grandchild of Kings'' at the Irish Repertory Theatre in February 1992,&lt;ref&gt;Collins, Glenn. &quot;Harold Prince Bound For Off Off Broadway, And Happy About It: Harold Prince Happily Bound for Off Off Broadway&quot;, ''The New York Times'', February 13, 1992, p. C21&lt;/ref&gt; receiving the 1992 [[Outer Critics Circle Award]] nomination for Best Actress.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.irishrep.org/awards.html &quot;Awards and Nominations, Irish Repertory Theatre&quot;] irishrep.org, accessed September 13, 2015&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.irishrep.org/ourhistory.html &quot;Our History&quot;] irishrep.org, accessed September 13, 2015&lt;/ref&gt; Other Off-Broadway work included ''Yeats: A Celebration''.&lt;ref name=play/&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{iobdb|Pauline|Flanagan}}&lt;/ref&gt; She appeared in the play ''Summer'', by [[Hugh Leonard]] at the Hudson Guild Theater, directed by Brian Murray. (''Summer'' premiered at the Olney Theatre, Maryland, in August 1974.)&lt;ref&gt;Leonard, Hugh. ''Summer: A Play'', Samuel French, Inc., 1979; ISBN 0573618585, p. 4&lt;/ref&gt;
==Death==
A resident of [[Glen Rock, New Jersey]], she died at [[The Valley Hospital]] in [[Ridgewood, New Jersey]] one day before her 78th birthday of [[heart failure]] following a battle with [[lung cancer]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://irishecho.com/?p=56098 &quot;Final Curtain&quot;], ''[[The Irish Echo]]'', 6 May 2003; accessed 17 September 2011; &quot;Actress Pauline Flanagan, one of the pillars of New York's Irish Repertory Theatre and 2001 winner of London's coveted Laurence Olivier Award, died in the early hours of last Saturday morning, after having suffered a massive stroke on June 23.... It was, in fact, in the midst of one of these sojourns, guest-starring in playwright [[Tom Stoppard]]'s ''Indian Ink'' at the Missouri Repertory Theatre in [[Kansas City, Missouri|Kansas City]] that the actress became sufficiently ill that she had to withdraw from the play a week before its closing performance and return to her home in [[Glen Rock, New Jersey]], only a few weeks ago.&quot;&lt;/ref&gt; She was survived by her husband, George Vogel (whom she married in 1958), a sister, Maura McNally, and her daughters Melissa Brown and Jane Holtzen.&lt;ref&gt;Hurley, Joseph. [http://www.independent.ie/regionals/sligochampion/news/pauline-flanagan-a-giant-of-new-yorks-theatrical-world-27539642.html &quot;Pauline Flanagan a giant of New York's theatrical world&quot;], independent.ie, 9 July 2003&lt;/ref&gt;
==Awards==
In 1997 she won the Barclays Theatre Awards&lt;!-- what award?? answered sept 13, 2015--&gt; for Best Actress in a Supporting Role for her role in [[Jennifer Johnston]]'s ''Desert Lullaby'', at the Lyric Theatre, Belfast.&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.britishtheatreguide.info/news/library/news/bl110297.htm &quot;British Theatre News&quot;] britishtheatreguide.info, 2 November, 1997&lt;/ref&gt; (The Barclays Theatre Awards are for outstanding regional theatre (including opera and dance) in the UK.&lt;ref&gt;Webb, Paul. [http://www.playbill.com/news/article/barclays-theatre-awards-shortlist-announced-98759# &quot;Barclays Theatre Awards Shortlist Announced&quot;], Playbill.com, 24 September 2001.&lt;/ref&gt;)
She was nominated for the 1982 [[Drama Desk Award]], Outstanding Featured Actress in a Play for ''Medea'' in which she performed on Broadway in 1982.&lt;ref&gt;[http://ibdb.com/show.php?id=5989 ''Medea'' 1982] ibdb, accessed September 13, 2015&lt;/ref&gt; In 2001 she won an [[Olivier Award]], Best Supporting Actress, for her performance in [[Frank McGuinness]]' ''[[Dolly West's Kitchen]]'' at the Old Vic.&lt;ref name=play/&gt;&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.olivierawards.com/winners &quot;Olivier Winners 2001&quot;] olivierawards.com/winners, accessed 14 September 2015.&lt;/ref&gt;

{{Infobox award
| name           = The Addy Awards
| image          = Addy Awards.jpg
| imagesize      = 150px
| caption        = Addy Awards logo
| description    = 
| presenter      = [[American Advertising Federation]]
| country        = 
| date           = 
| website        = [http://www.americanadvertisingawards.com/]
}}
The '''American Advertising Awards''', formerly the ADDY Awards, is the advertising industry's largest and most representative competition, attracting more than 40,000 entries every year in local [[American Advertising Federation]] Club (Ad Club) competitions. The mission of the American Advertising Awards competition is to recognize and reward the creative spirit of excellence in the art of advertising.
Conducted annually by the [[American Advertising Federation (AAF)]], the local Ad Club phase is the first of a three-tier, national competition. Concurrently, all across the country, local entrants vie to win ADDY Awards - recognition as the very best in their markets. At the second tier, local ADDY winners compete against winners from other local clubs in one of 15 district competitions. District ADDY winners are then forwarded to the third tier, the national stage of the American Advertising Awards. Entry in your local Ad Club competition is the first step toward winning a national ADDY.
Entering the American Advertising Awards supports the entire advertising industry, because the AAF and its local and district affiliates use the proceeds to enhance advertising through programs such as public service, internships, advocacy groups, advertising education, and consumer awareness.
Selection of the most creative entry in each category is affected by a scoring process in which a panel of judges evaluates all creative dimensions of every entry. A GOLD ADDY is recognition of the highest level of creative excellence and is judged to be superior to all other entries in the category. Entries that are also considered outstanding and worthy of recognition receive a SILVER ADDY. The number of awards given in each category is determined by the judges, based on the relative quality of work in that category.&lt;ref&gt;http://www.americanadvertisingawards.com/&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:Cushing QC 1.JPG|thumb|right|250px|Cushing]]
[[File:Greeces Point QC.JPG|thumb|right|250px|Greece's Point]]
'''Brownsburg-Chatham''' is a municipality in the [[Laurentides]] region of Quebec, Canada, part of the [[Argenteuil Regional County Municipality]]. It is located along the [[Ottawa River]], just west of [[Lachute]], and includes the communities of Brownsburg, Cushing, Dalesville, Greece's Point, Pine Hill, and Saint-Philippe. Economic activities include agriculture, tourism and the manufacture of explosives.
It is the hometown of [[Gilles Lupien]], retired defenceman of the [[Montreal Canadiens]].
==History==
Chatham Township was established in 1799, and named after English Statesman [[William Pitt, 1st Earl of Chatham]] (1708–1778). Mostly populated by rich American [[Loyalist (American Revolution)|Loyalists]], its post office opened in 1829 and the township municipality was formed in 1845. By the mid-19th century, there were a few hamlets including Dalesville, Brownsbury, and Starneville.&lt;ref name=&quot;toponymie&quot;&gt;{{cite web |url=http://www.toponymie.gouv.qc.ca/CT/toposweb/Fiche.aspx?no_seq=375817 |title=Brownsburg-Chatham (Ville) |accessdate=2010-06-09 |publisher=Commission de toponymie du Québec |language=French}}&lt;/ref&gt;
In 1818, Major George Brown, born in England, was granted by the government land on the West River (''Rivière de l'Ouest''). Together with pioneers Daniel Smith and Captain A. L. Howard, he was able to breathe life into the local industry during the 1820s. George Brown built, among other achievements, a sawmill and gristmill which contributed to the economic growth of the region and led to the formation of Brownsburg village which was named after him. In 1854, the Brownsburg Post Office opened. Later in the third quarter of the 19th century, a major arms factory run by the [[Canadian Industries Limited|Dominion Cartridge Company]] was the major local industry.&lt;ref name=&quot;toponymie&quot;/&gt;
In 1935, the Village Municipality of Brownsburg was officially established by separating from the Township Municipality of Chatham.&lt;ref name=&quot;toponymie&quot;/&gt;
On October 6, 1999, Brownsburg was rejoined with Chatham and the new Municipality of Brownsburg-Chatham was formed. On July 6, 2002, the municipality changed its statutes and became the Town of Brownsburg-Chatham.&lt;ref name=&quot;toponymie&quot;/&gt;

    <title>List of special elections to the United States House of Representatives</title>

      <model>wikitext</model>
      <format>text/x-wiki</format>
      <text xml:space="preserve">Below is a list of '''special elections''' to the [[United States House of Representatives]]. Such elections are called by [[Governor (United States)|state governors]] to fill vacancies that occur when a member of the [[United States House of Representatives|House of Representatives]] dies or resigns before the biennial [[general election]]. Winners of these elections serve the remainder of the term and are usually candidates in the next general election for their districts.
In the United States, these contests have been called &quot;special elections&quot; because they do do occur on [[Election Day (United States)|Election Day]] like regular congressional elections.  Despite their name, however, special elections to the U.S. House happen quite often.  Furthermore, one published study shows that special elections are explained by the same factors as regular congressional elections.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal | last1=Knotts | first1=H. Gibbs | last2=Ragusa | first2=Jordan | date=2016 | title=The Nationalization of Special Elections for the U.S. House of Representatives | url=http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/17457289.2015.1063497 | journal=Journal of Elections, Public Opinion, and Parties | publisher=Taylor &amp; Francis | volume=26 | issue=1 | pages=22–39 | doi=10.1080/17457289.2015.1063497 }}&lt;/ref&gt; Special elections to the U.S. House of Representatives have occurred at least once in all states except Iowa and Idaho. A few special elections for territorial delegates to Congress have also been held.
